epigenomes</h1></div><div class="authors u-clearfix" data-component="SpringerLink.Authors"><ul class="u-interface u-inline-list authors__title" </div></div><section class="Abstract" id="Abs1" tabindex="-1" lang="en"><h2 class="Heading">Abstract</h2><p class="Para">Epigenetic alterations are associated with all aspects of cancer, from tumor initiation to cancer progression and metastasis. It is now well understood that both losses and gains of DNA methylation as well as altered chromatin organization contribute significantly to cancer-associated phenotypes. More recently, new sequencing technologies have allowed the identification of driver mutations in epigenetic regulators, providing a mechanistic link between the cancer epigenome and genetic alterations. Oncogenic activating mutations are now known to occur in a number of epigenetic modifiers (i.e. <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH1</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">2</em>, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EZH2</em>, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">DNMT3A</em>), pinpointing epigenetic pathways that are involved in tumorigenesis. Similarly, investigations into the role of inactivating mutations in chromatin modifiers (i.e. <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM6A</em>, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">CREBBP</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EP300</em>, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCB1</em>) implicate many of these genes as tumor suppressors. Intriguingly, a number of neoplasms are defined by a plethora of mutations in epigenetic regulators, including renal, bladder, and adenoid cystic carcinomas. Particularly striking is the discovery of frequent histone H3.3 mutations in pediatric glioma, a particularly aggressive neoplasm that has long remained poorly understood. Cancer epigenetics is a relatively new, promising frontier with much potential for improving cancer outcomes. Already, therapies such as 5-azacytidine and decitabine have proven that targeting epigenetic alterations in cancer can lead to tangible benefits. Understanding how genetic alterations give rise to the cancer epigenome will offer new possibilities for developing better prognostic and therapeutic strategies.</p></section><div class="KeywordGroup" lang="en"><h3 class="Heading">Keywords</h3><span class="Keyword">chromatin </span><span class="Keyword">cancer </span><span class="Keyword">epigenetics </span><span class="Keyword">mutations </span><span class="Keyword">methylation </span></div><div class="AbbreviationGroup"><h3 class="Heading">Abbreviations</h3><div class="DefinitionList"><dl><dt class="Term">ACC</dt><dd class="Description"><p class="Para">adenoid cystic carcinoma</p></dd><dt class="Term">AML</dt><dd class="Description"><p class="Para">acute myeloid leukemia</p></dd><dt class="Term">CGI</dt><dd class="Description"><p class="Para">CpG island</p></dd><dt class="Term">HNPCC</dt><dd class="Description"><p class="Para">hereditary nonpolyposis colon cancer</p></dd><dt class="Term">HDACs</dt><dd class="Description"><p class="Para">histone deacetylatases</p></dd><dt class="Term">MSI</dt><dd class="Description"><p class="Para">microsatellite instability</p></dd><dt class="Term">TCC</dt><dd class="Description"><p class="Para">transitional cell carcinoma</p></dd></dl></div></div><div class="note test-pdf-link" id="cobranding-and-download-availability-text"><div>    <a class="gtm-pdf-link" href="/content/pdf/10.1007%2Fs13238-014-0031-6.pdf" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Download</a>
 fulltext PDF</div></div><div class="article-actions--inline" id="article-actions--inline" data-component="article-actions--inline"></div><div id="body"><section id="Sec1" tabindex="-1" class="Section1 RenderAsSection1 SectionTypeIntroduction"><h2 class="Heading">INTRODUCTION</h2><div class="content"><p class="Para">Cancer is an evolutionary process through which dysregulation in select cellular mechanisms confers a clonal advantage, leading to tumor growth and eventually, metastasis. In fact, nearly all cancers are defined by several “hallmark” capabilities, including resisting cell death, evading growth suppressors, uncontrolled proliferation, neoangiogenesis, invasion/metastasis, and replicative immortality (Hanahan and Weinberg, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR115">2011</a></span>). Aberrant control of other processes, such as defective differentiation and DNA damage repair, is also linked to tumor formation. Research efforts have traditionally focused on genetic abnormalities underlying malignant transformation, due to initial technological constraints and our limited understanding of other heritable patterns of gene regulation. Early studies on these genetic alterations—copy number variations, mutations, gene rearrangements—have defined mechanisms of oncogenesis, led to the creation of targeted therapies, and improved patient outcomes for certain cancers. Only recently has it become evident that many genetic alterations in cancer target epigenetic regulators, causing cancer-associated phenotypes via epigenetic dysfunction.</p><div class="Para">Since the first discovery of cancer-associated loss of DNA methylation, the field of cancer epigenetics has grown remarkably and helped elucidate aspects of cancer biology where genetic explanations alone are insufficient (Feinberg and Vogelstein, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR79">1983</a></span>). Epigenetics is the process by which cells encode non-genetic, heritable information through alterations that do not change the DNA sequence. Generally, chromatin exists in two main forms—condensed, transcriptional silent heterochromatin and euchromatin, which is transcriptionally active. The functional unit of chromatin is the nucleosome, which is an octameric structure composed of two histones each of H2A, H2B, H3, and H4 encircled by 147 bp of DNA (Margueron and Reinberg, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR204">2010</a></span>). Regulation of the chromatin state is achieved through DNA methylation, chromatin remodeling, and/or covalent histone modifications, such as methylation, acetylation, phosphorylation, ubiquitination, and sumoylation. The major effectors of these modifications are the chromatin modifier enzymes, highly specific proteins that catalyze the addition or removal of functional groups to DNA or histone tails. These modifications alter chromatin structure through noncovalent interactions within and between nucleosomes, leading to changes in macromolecular organization and promoter accessibility. In addition, these chromatin “marks” serve as signals to other specialized proteins involved in chromatin organization, gene transcription, genome maintenance, and replication (Kouzarides, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR167">2007</a></span>; Sharma et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR284">2010</a></span>). As a result, aberrations in one or more of these modifiers can have profound effects on normal cell physiology and are now well-documented in many diseases, including cancer (Fig. <span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig1">1</a></span>).<figure class="Figure" id="Fig1"><div class="MediaObject"><a href="https://static-content.springer.com/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs13238-014-0031-6/MediaObjects/13238_2014_31_Fig1_HTML.gif" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"><span class="u-screenreader-only">Open image in new window</span><img src="https://static-content.springer.com/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs13238-014-0031-6/MediaObjects/13238_2014_31_Fig1_HTML.gif" alt="" /></a></div><figcaption class="Caption" lang="en"><div class="CaptionContent"><span class="CaptionNumber">Figure 1</span><p class="SimplePara"><strong class="EmphasisTypeBold ">Epigenetic regulation of gene expression</strong>. Epigenetic processes include DNA methylation, covalent histone modifications (e.g. methylation, acetylation), and chromatin remodeling (SWI/SNF complex). Modifier proteins with frequent driver mutations in cancer are shown by specific function and target site. Green and red font colors represent histone “writers” and “erasers,” respectively. Lightning bolts represent cancer-associated mutations in histones H3.1 (K27) and H3.3 (K27, G34)</p></div></figcaption></figure></div><p class="Para">It has been known for decades that epigenetic dysregulation occurs in cancer. Aberrant DNA hyper- and hypo-methylation was one of the first molecular features noted to be present in cancer cells (Feinberg and Vogelstein, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR79">1983</a></span>; Gama-Sosa et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR92">1983</a></span>; Rubery and Newton, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR271">1973</a></span>; Timp and Feinberg, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR314">2013</a></span>). However, the nature of epigenetic alterations and the molecular underpinnings of these changes are just beginning to be appreciated. The recent accelerated search for mutations in cancers, facilitated through next generation sequencing technologies, has greatly accelerated our understanding of the mechanisms underlying epigenetic dysfunction in cancers.</p><p class="Para">Genome-wide and exomic sequencing data from recent years have demonstrated that mutations in epigenetic modifiers comprise a large portion of all genetic events in many cancers, including tumors such as renal carcinoma, adenoid cystic carcinoma (ACC), and transitional cell carcinoma (TCC) of the bladder (Dalgliesh et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR56">2010</a></span>; Gui et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR109">2011</a></span>; Ho et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR124">2013</a></span>). In fact, the main objective of these recent genomic analyses is the identification of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">bona fide</em> “driver” mutations in cancer genes. These mutations are defined by their ability to promote or “drive” tumorigenesis and are therefore positively selected for in the development of cancer. In contrast, “passenger” mutations, which comprise the majority of mutations identified, represent genetic events that have no direct or indirect effect on the selective growth advantage of the cell in which it occurred (Stratton et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR301">2009</a></span>; Vogelstein et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR332">2013</a></span>). Candidate epigenetic driver genes have been identified as either mutated across many cancers (e.g. <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM6A</em> in over 12 cancers), having highly recurrent mutations (e.g. <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH1</em>R132), or having highly prevalent mutations in select tumor histologies (e.g. <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MLL2</em> in follicular lymphoma) (You and Jones, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR372">2012</a></span>). The characterization of these driver gene mutations has enhanced our understanding of the mechanisms contributing to oncogenesis, allowed for improved prognostic assessment, and opened the door for the development of exciting new therapies. In this review, we highlight recent developments, new discoveries, and therapeutic advances involving cancer-associated mutations in epigenetic regulators.</p></div></section><section id="Sec2" tabindex="-1" class="Section1 RenderAsSection1"><h2 class="Heading">DNA METHYLATION AND HYDROXYMETHYLATION</h2><div class="content"><p class="Para">DNA methylation plays a well-defined role in both development and disease, including cancer. First identified in 1975, CpG island (CGI) methylation was shown to function as a relatively stable alteration on DNA that can serve to silence gene transcription (Holliday and Pugh, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR125">1975</a></span>; Riggs, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR264">1975</a></span>). We now understand that DNA methylation is much more dynamic and complex, with diverse epigenetic consequences linked to varied genomic locations of where this mark occurs. For example, DNA methylation at gene promoter CGIs potently blocks the initiation of transcription, whereas methylation within CpG-poor gene bodies may actually facilitate elongation and influence patterns of alternate splicing. In addition, DNA methylation is frequently found in repeat-rich areas of the genome and is vital for both chromosomal and genomic stability, possibly through the repression of retroviral transposons (Jones, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR144">2012</a></span>). Still, the role for this epigenetic mark at other regulatory regions, such as enhancers and insulators, has yet to be determined. Regardless, aberrant methylation in human cancer is a defining feature, with global promoter CGI hypermethylation and non-CGI hypomethylation widely reported (Ehrlich, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR72">2002</a></span>; Sharma et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR284">2010</a></span>). Furthermore, local variations in methylation at only several key loci have been shown to be sufficient for tumorigenesis (Lee et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR176">2008</a></span>; Poage et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR253">2011</a></span>). Importantly, these altered patterns of DNA epigenetic marks (e.g. 5-mC, 5-hmC) are frequently accompanied by a critical imbalance in transcriptional programs involving differentiation and stem cell maintenance, thereby initiating tumorigenesis and sustaining growth (Jones and Baylin, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR146">2007</a></span>).</p><p class="Para">DNA methylation can function to silence tumor suppressor genes along with genetic mutations (Herman and Baylin, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR121">2003</a></span>). For example, in the case of hereditary gastric cancer, methylation of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">CDH1</em> (which encodes the E-cadherin tumor suppressor) can function as a “second hit” and cause gastric cancer when the first allele is mutated (Grady et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR100">2000</a></span>). In sporadic cancers, tumor suppressor genes that are mutated in hereditary versions of the disease are frequently silenced by DNA methylation instead (Esteller et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR77">2001</a></span>). For example, in hereditary nonpolyposis colon cancer (HNPCC), <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MLH1</em> inactivation via mutation can lead to microsatellite instability (MSI) and tumorigenesis, whereas in sporadic colon cancers, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MLH1</em> is frequently silenced by methylation (Kane et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR152">1997</a></span>; Veigl et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR328">1998</a></span>). These data and others indicate that aberrant DNA methylation can work along with genetic alterations to promote tumorigenesis.</p><section id="Sec3" tabindex="-1" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2"><h3 class="Heading">DNMT3A</h3><p class="Para">DNA methylation is carried out by the mammalian DNA methyltransferases (DNMTs), essential enzymes that catalyze the addition of a methyl group to cytosine in CpG dinucleotides in DNA (Jones, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR144">2012</a></span>). The conversion of 5-cytosine (5-C) to methyl-cytosine (5-mC) requires the presence of a methyl donor, S-adenosylmethionine (SAM), and one of the following catalytically active DNMTs: DNMT1, DNMT3A, or DNMT3B (Shen et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR285">1992</a></span>). Although there is some evidence for overlapping roles, DNMT3A and DNMT3B are essential for <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">de novo</em> DNA methylation, whereas DNMT1 “maintains” heritable methylation patterns across the genome during cell replication (Hsieh, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR129">1999</a></span>). In fact, the role of DNMT3A in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">de novo</em> methylation was initially characterized in the context of epigenetic silencing during development, including at imprinted loci in germ cells (Kaneda et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR153">2004</a></span>; Okano et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR233">1999</a></span>). The DNMTs have long been suspected to play a role in oncogenesis, as well. Aberrations in DNA methylation—both hyper- and hypo-methylation—have been well-documented in patient tumors and cell lines for decades (El-Osta, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR74">2004</a></span>). Additionally, Oka and colleagues first showed that in some cases, DNMT3A and DNMT3B, not DNMT1, mediate the cytotoxic effects of 5-aza-dC, a therapeutic mainstay in the treatment of several hematopoietic malignancies (Oka et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR232">2005</a></span>; Plimack et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR252">2007</a></span>). Not long after, DNMT3A and DNMT3B were further implicated in both hematopoietic stem cell (HSC) renewal and differentiation, two tightly regulated processes whose perturbation can lead to carcinogenesis (Challen et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR37">2012</a></span>; Tadokoro et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR304">2007</a></span>).</p><p class="Para"><em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">DNMT3A</em> somatic mutations were first discovered by Yamashita and colleagues following sequencing of tissue from adult patients with <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">de novo</em> acute myeloid leukemia (AML) (Yamashita et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR361">2010</a></span>). Soon after, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">DNMT3A</em> mutations were reported in AML cohorts from others, with frequencies as high as 22.1% (Ley et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR178">2010</a></span>). The majority of mutations occur at R882 (60%–64%), and almost all are heterozygous (Ley et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR178">2010</a></span>; Thol et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR312">2011a</a></span>). <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">DNMT3A</em> mutations are enriched in AML patients with intermediate-risk cytogenetics and normal karyotype (Lin et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR185">2011</a></span>; Patel et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR243">2012</a></span>). They are also associated with increased age, M4 and M5 AML subtypes, worse overall survival (OS) and relapse-free survival (RFS), and increased blasts at diagnosis (Hou et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR128">2012</a></span>; Ley et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR178">2010</a></span>; Lin et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR185">2011</a></span>; Marcucci et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR202">2012</a></span>; Thol et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR312">2011a</a></span>; Yan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR364">2011</a></span>). There is also evidence that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">DNTM3A</em> mutations may be a prognostic marker for decreased time to treatment failure (TTF), duration of complete remission (CR), and disease-free survival (DFS), at least in cytogenetically normal AML (Marcucci et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR202">2012</a></span>; Thol et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR312">2011a</a></span>; Yan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR364">2011</a></span>). These studies have also identified several frequently co-occurring mutations, which include <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">NPM1</em>, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">FLT3</em>, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH1</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">2</em>, and less commonly, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> (Hou et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR128">2012</a></span>; Ley et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR178">2010</a></span>; Lin et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR185">2011</a></span>; Marcucci et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR202">2012</a></span>; Renneville et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR262">2012</a></span>; Thol et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR312">2011a</a></span>; Yan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR364">2011</a></span>). As alterations in epigenetic regulators frequently lead to genomic instability, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">DNMT3A</em> mutation may further drive progression of the disease itself (Wakita et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR333">2013</a></span>). Further, recent experimental work has demonstrated that DNMT3A mutations at R882 are only found in major clones, suggesting this genetic alteration may be an initiating lesion in AML (Bisling et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR16">2013</a></span>).</p><p class="Para"><em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">DNMT3A</em> mutations have also been described, albeit at lower frequency, in other myeloid malignancies, such as myelodysplastic syndromes (MDS; 3%–8%) and myeloproliferative neoplasms (MPNs; 2%–10%), as well as early T-cell precursor acute lymphoblastic leukemia (ETP-ALL; 16%–18%) (Brecqueville et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR27">2011</a></span>; Ewalt et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR78">2011</a></span>; Grossmann et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR106">2013</a></span>; Neumann et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR221">2013</a></span>; Stegelmann et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR299">2011</a></span>; Thol et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR313">2011b</a></span>; Traina et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR315">2013</a></span>; Walter et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR334">2011</a></span>). Similar to what is observed in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">de novo</em> AML, R882 is most frequently targeted for mutation (60%) in these neoplasms (Thol et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR313">2011b</a></span>). Clinically, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">DNMT3A</em> mutations also correlated with increased age and predicted prognosis in all types, including worse OS, event-free survival (EFS), and AML-free survival (Lin et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR185">2011</a></span>; Neumann et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR221">2013</a></span>; Renneville et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR262">2012</a></span>; Thol et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR313">2011b</a></span>; Walter et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR334">2011</a></span>). Paradoxically, univariate and multivariate analysis of 92 patients with MDS revealed <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">DNMT3A</em> mutations were correlated with better overall response rate (ORR) and progression-free survival (PFS) (Traina et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR315">2013</a></span>). Interestingly, similar to AML, MPNs showed an association between <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">DNMT3A</em> alterations and mutation in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">JAK2</em>, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH1</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">2</em>, and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ASXL1</em> but not <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> (Stegelmann et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR299">2011</a></span>). However, studies in MDS and ETP-ALL have found no association between <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">DNMT3A</em> mutations and other known leukemogenic drivers, including <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">FLT3</em> (Neumann et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR221">2013</a></span>; Thol et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR313">2011b</a></span>).</p><p class="Para">DNMT3A is a 102 kDa protein with three highly conserved functional domains: An N-terminal PWWP domain, a cysteine-rich PHD zinc finger domain, and a C-terminal catalytic domain (Hermann et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR122">2004</a></span>). <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">DNMT3A</em> mutations in cancer have been reported in all three domains, with most occurring in the catalytic domain, including the R882 mutation (60%) (Ley et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR178">2010</a></span>). Still, many cancer-specific mutations occur in non-catalytic domains. The PWWP domain is essential in localizing DNMT3A to heterochromatic regions of DNA during interphase, though it is unclear if this is related to its reported ability to bind DNA directly (Bachman et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR7">2001</a></span>; Ge et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR95">2004</a></span>; Purdy et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR257">2010</a></span>; Suetake et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR303">2011</a></span>). Alternatively, the PHD domain has also been shown to mediate regional specificity and repression through its interactions with transcriptional repressor RP58, HP1beta, histone deacetylatases (HDACs), and SUV39H1 (Datta et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR59">2003</a></span>; Fuks et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR89">2001</a></span>; Fuks et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR90">2003</a></span>). Exactly how these mutations disrupt protein function is of great interest, although a single unifying mechanism is unlikely to exist. Likewise, a recent clinical study found that R882 mutations confer poor prognosis in older populations, whereas non-R882 mutations confer poor prognosis in younger patients (Marcucci et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR202">2012</a></span>).</p><p class="Para">Whether these reported mutations are loss-of-function, gain-of-function, or act via a dominant-negative mechanism has also been debated. A strong case can be made that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">DNMT3A</em> is an oncogene, as it is overexpressed in several cancers, depletion results in decreased proliferation and metastasis, and 5-aza-dC causes apoptosis through direct inhibition of DNMT3A (Oka et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR232">2005</a></span>). In regards to mutations themselves, the R882H DNMT3A mutant was sufficient to promote tumorigenesis in an IL-3 dependent transformation assay in leukemic 32D cells (Yan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR364">2011</a></span>). Alternatively, some studies have demonstrated that mutant DNMT3A (R882H) has a markedly reduced catalytic ability (~50%) in methyltransferase assays and decreased DNA-binding capacity <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">in vitro</em>, implying a possible loss-of-function phenotype via a dominant-negative mechanism (Jia et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR143">2007</a></span>; Ley et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR178">2010</a></span>; Yamashita et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR361">2010</a></span>). Clearly, additional studies are necessary to understand the exact nature by which these cancer-associated mutations are transforming.</p><p class="Para">Characterizing larger DNMT3A-induced changes in the cancer methylome has proven quite challenging. Ley and colleagues showed no changes in genome-wide methylation according to <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">DNMT3A</em> status in AML, and although 182 specific regions showed increased hypomethylation in mutant samples, this did not correlate with gene expression (Ley et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR178">2010</a></span>). However, in a more recent cohort, a total of 3878 genomic regions were found to have significantly different methylation patterns using MeDIP-chip and differences of expression levels in 889 of 20,723 annotated genes was observed via an Affymetrix microarray (Yan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR364">2011</a></span>). Further, this group found during RT-PCR validation that the expression of several HOX family genes significantly increased in DNMT3A mutant samples compared to wild-type.</p><p class="Para">Recent experiments may offer some insight behind the conflicting methylation and functional data. Protein binding at the DNMT3A tetramerization interface is important for methylation patterning, inducing processive methylation of clustered sites (Jia et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR143">2007</a></span>). Most mutations, including R882, occur within this tetramer interface. Therefore, differences between oligomerization states can explain how <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">DNMT3A</em> mutations alter epigenetic silencing and lead to transformation, without global changes in DNA methylation (Holz-Schietinger et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR126">2011</a></span>; Holz-Schietinger et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR127">2012</a></span>). Although the commonly occurring R882H mutation does not disrupt DNMT3A association with required cofactor DNMT3L <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">in vitro</em>, the latter is only expressed in early development (Chedin et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR41">2002</a></span>; Jia et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR143">2007</a></span>; Webster et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR346">2005</a></span>; Yamashita et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR361">2010</a></span>). However, other binding proteins, such as thymine-DNA glycosylase and ecotropic viral intergration site 1, can adhere to sites within the DNMT3A catalytic domain and may explain altered mutant DNMT3A activity (Li et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR179">2007</a></span>; Senyuk et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR281">2011</a></span>). In addition, non-catalytic mutations may disrupt protein binding to other domains, as described above. Although the exact mechanism remains elusive, these mutations result in decreased methylation processivity and altered localization, possibly to euchromatic regions of DNA (Jurkowska et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR149">2011</a></span>). This may help explain how <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">DNMT3A</em> mutations drive tumor formation in hematopoietic malignancies, even in the absence of larger global methylation changes.</p></section><section id="Sec4" tabindex="-1" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2"><h3 class="Heading">TET2</h3><p class="Para">The ten-eleven translocation (TET) family proteins were first discovered in cancer with the fusion of TET1 to MLL in select AML patients with t(10;11)(q22;q23) (Lorsbach et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR189">2003</a></span>). Mechanistic studies then showed the TET proteins are dioxygenases that depend on 2-oxoglutarate, oxygen, Fe(II), and ascorbic acid to catalyze the conversion of 5-mC to 5-hydroxymethylcytosine (5-hmC) at CpG regions in DNA (Blaschke et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR18">2013</a></span>; Ito et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR137">2010</a></span>; Minor et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR211">2013</a></span>; Tahiliani et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR305">2009</a></span>; Yin et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR369">2013</a></span>). TET enzymes may be responsible for DNA demethylation through both passive and active means. For example, CpG dinucleotides that are “marked” with 5-hmC are not recognized by DNMT1 and therefore, methylation is passively lost at these sites through repeated cycles of cell division (Valinluck and Sowers, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR323">2007</a></span>). Alternatively, active demethylation can proceed following placement of 5-hmC via the activation-induced cytidine deaminase (AID)-APOBEC DNA repair pathway (Guo et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR112">2011</a></span>). More recently, an even greater role for TET enzymes in active demethylation was shown <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">in vitro</em>, with TET enzymes proving sufficient for converting 5-mC to 5-hmC, 5-hmC to 5-formylcytosine (5-fC), and finally 5-fC to 5-carboxylcytosine (5-caC). 5-caC is then targeted by base excision repair enzymes to complete the demethylation process (He et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR118">2011</a></span>; Ito et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR138">2011</a></span>).</p><p class="Para"><em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> was first suspected to have a role in cancer when six patients with either secondary AML (sAML) or MDS were noted to have minimal deletions via FISH on chromosome 4q24 (Viguie et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR331">2005</a></span>). Soon after, the first <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> somatic mutations were identified in 25 patients (14%) with MPNs (Delhommeau et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR61">2008</a></span>). Delhommeau and colleages then sequenced <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> in patient tumor samples, becoming the first group to identify <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> mutations in multiple myeloid neoplasms, including MDS (19%), MPNs (12%–14%), and sAML (24%). They concluded that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> was a novel <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">bona fide</em> tumor suppressor, noting that the majority of mutations are heterozygous (55%) and that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> defects precede the well-known JAK2 V617F driver mutation in MPN HSCs (Delhommeau et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR62">2009</a></span>). More recently, Schaub and colleagues disputed this result using colony formation assays to show that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> mutations can either precede (4 of 8 patients), follow (2 of 8), or occur independently (2 of 8) of JAK2 V617F mutations in MPN patient samples (Schaub et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR277">2010</a></span>). Although the temporal relationship between <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> mutations and other leukemogenic drivers is still unclear, the frequency and ubiquitous nature of these mutations in cancer is quite revealing. In addition to MDS, sAML, and MPNs, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> mutations have now been described in other myeloid neoplasms, such as <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">de novo</em> AML (12%) and chronic myelomonocytic leukemia (CMML; 42%–46%) (Abdel-Wahab et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR1">2009</a></span>; Smith et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR296">2010</a></span>).</p><p class="Para">Unlike <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">DNMT3A</em> mutations, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> alterations seem to hold limited prognostic utility in leukemia. The overwhelming majority of studies published to date have found no change in OS or any other prognostic tools between patients harboring <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> mutations and those who are not. However, one study in a cohort of 96 MDS patients reported that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> mutations conferred an OS, EFS, and AML-free survival advantage (Kosmider et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR166">2009</a></span>). Paradoxically, Abdel-Wahab and colleagues found that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> mutations were linked to worse OS in 93 patients with <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">de novo</em> AML, and a recent whole-exome sequencing study reported worse EFS in AML patients with <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> mutation (Abdel-Wahab et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR1">2009</a></span>; Weissmann et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR347">2012</a></span>). Further, Nibourel and colleagues reported no association with OS or DFS in their cohort of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">de novo</em> AML patients, though the prevalence of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> mutations in patients who failed to achieve complete remission (CR) trended higher (27% vs. 17%) (Nibourel et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR224">2010</a></span>). Despite questionable association to patient outcomes, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> mutations are linked with other clinical features, including monocytosis, leukocytosis, and advanced age at diagnosis (Jankowska et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR142">2009</a></span>; Smith et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR296">2010</a></span>; Tefferi et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR307">2009a</a></span>; Tefferi et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR309">2009c</a></span>). Although <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> mutations show little association to known myeloid leukemogenic drivers<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">—FLT3-ITD, RUNX1, CEBPA—</em>they do associate with <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">NPM1</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ASXL1</em> mutations and infrequently co-occur with <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH1</em> or <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH2</em> mutations (Chou et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR49">2011a</a></span>; Weissmann et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR347">2012</a></span>). Lastly, despite some data indicating no association between cytogenetics and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> status in MPN, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> mutations occur more often in the presence of normal karyotype and intermediate-risk AML (Hussein et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR133">2010</a></span>; Weissmann et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR347">2012</a></span>). In this cytogenetic setting, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> mutations do predict significantly worse OS in AML (Chou et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR49">2011a</a></span>).</p><p class="Para">Although few <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> recurrent mutations have been reported, many mutations result in a frameshift or early stop codon and are therefore inactivating (Tefferi et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR308">2009b</a></span>). In fact, the largest proportion of nonsense mutations occur in exon 3, resulting in a truncated protein lacking the C-terminal catalytic domain (Moran-Crusio et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR214">2011</a></span>). Additionally, several missense mutations have been characterized as loss-of-function, with Ko and colleagues reporting impaired hydroxylation of 5-mC when mutant TET2 was overexpressed in HEK-293T cells. Furthermore, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> mutation status is significantly correlated with decreased global 5-hmC in myeloid tumors (Ko et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR163">2010</a></span>; Konstandin et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR165">2011</a></span>). Functional studies manipulating <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> have also been able to recapitulate phenotypes that are characteristic of myeloid neoplasms, suggesting that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> loss may be a key event in leukemic transformation. For example, a conditional mouse model for <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> loss in the hematopoietic compartment resulted in increased HSC self-renewal and myeloproliferation including splenomegaly, monocytosis, and extramedullary hematopoiesis (Moran-Crusio et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR214">2011</a></span>). This is consistent with other studies showing that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> inactivation leads to decreased 5-hmC in HSCs, amplification of the stem cell population, and may skew HSCs toward a myeloid lineage (Pronier et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR255">2011</a></span>; Quivoron et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR258">2011</a></span>).</p><p class="Para">The true effect of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> mutations on DNA methylation status has been difficult to ascertain. Despite an expected increase in 5-mC following <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> inactivation, several studies have reported a global decrease in methylation (Ko et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR163">2010</a></span>; Yamazaki et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR362">2012</a></span>). However, analysis of specific gene promoters shows mixed results in TET2 mutant samples, frequently exhibiting promoter-specific hypermethylation in spite of global hypomethylation (Perez et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR248">2012</a></span>; Wu et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR356">2011</a></span>; Yamazaki et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR362">2012</a></span>). Still, Ko and colleagues noted that several AML patients with wild-type TET2 had 5-hmC levels very similar to those patients with mutant TET2 (Ko et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR163">2010</a></span>). This suggests a more complex relationship between <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em>, DNA methylation status, and malignant transformation.</p><p class="Para">In the past year, exciting new evidence has emerged to suggest a more diverse role for <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> in epigenetic regulation. In addition to known associations with polycomb repressive complex (PRC) regulator SIN3A and NURD complex member MBD3 (Wu et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR356">2011</a></span>; Yildirim et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR368">2011</a></span>), TET2 was recently identified as a direct binding partner with O-linked beta-N-acetylglucosamine transferase (OGT), an enzyme that marks histone H2B S112 at active transcription start sites (TSS) (Chen et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR43">2013b</a></span>). Although OGT doesn’t influence TET2 activity in functional assays, TET2 seems to actively target OGT to unmethylated promoters and activate transcription via other means (Vella et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR329">2013</a></span>). Furthermore, Deplus and colleagues showed that TET2 and OGT co-localize at active promoters marked by H3K4me3 through a direct interaction with host-cell factor 1 (HCF1) and that knockdown of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> leads to global decreases of H3K4me3 and GlcNAcylation (Deplus et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR63">2013</a></span>). Another direct interaction has been described between TET2 and EBF1, a transcription factor that is associated with transcriptional activation and “poised” chromatin (Guilhamon et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR111">2013</a></span>; Treiber et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR316">2010</a></span>). Additional binding partners have also been reported within the past year, including NANOG and IDAX (Costa et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR53">2013</a></span>; Ko et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR164">2013</a></span>). Lastly, several novel miRNAs were discovered to negatively regulate <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> expression, offering a possible explanation for <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET</em>-associated transformation in the absence of any genomic alterations (Cheng et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR45">2013</a></span>; Fu et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR87">2013</a></span>; Song et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR298">2013</a></span>). Collectively, evidence is mounting that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> inactivation in cancer may alter more than just DNA methylation; in fact, transformation may result considerably from disrupted interactions with other epigenetic regulators and development-associated transcription factors.</p></section><section id="Sec5" tabindex="-1" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2"><h3 class="Heading">IDH1/2</h3><p class="Para">Isocitrate dehydrogenase 1 (IDH1) and isocitrate dehydrogenase 2 (IDH2) are two homodimeric metabolic enzymes that convert isocitrate to α-ketoglutarate (α-KG) while reducing NADP<sup>+</sup> to NADPH. IDH1 is present in the cytosol and peroxisomes whereas IDH2 is located exclusively in mitochondria (Geisbrecht and Gould, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR96">1999</a></span>; Winkler et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR353">1986</a></span>; Xu et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR359">2004</a></span>). Frequent recurrent mutations in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH1</em> were initially discovered in GBM (12%) following whole-exome sequencing of 22 patient tumor samples (Parsons et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR238">2008</a></span>). Further sequencing efforts revealed that mutations are most prevalent in WHO grade II/III gliomas (71%) and secondary GBMs (88%) but less common in primary GBMs (7%) (Balss et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR10">2008</a></span>). Subsequent studies have shown that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH2</em> mutations are also enriched in WHO grade II/III gliomas, albeit less frequently, and that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH1</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">2</em> mutations occur in a mutually exclusive manner (Hartmann et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR117">2009</a></span>). These data indicate <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> mutation is an early event in glioma oncogenesis, frequently preceding known alterations like <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TP53</em> mutation and 1p/19q loss (Watanabe et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR344">2009</a></span>). Interestingly, recent data suggest <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH1</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH2</em> mutations may actually differentially associate with astrocytoma and oligodendrogliomas, respectively (Hartmann et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR117">2009</a></span>). <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> mutations are associated with <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MGMT</em> promoter hypermethylation, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TP53</em> mutation, 1p/19q codeletion, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ATRX</em> inactivation, younger age, and improved prognosis while being inversely correlated with <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EGFR</em> amplification in glioma (Chou et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR48">2010</a></span>; Wiestler et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR349">2013</a></span>; Yan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR363">2009</a></span>; Zou et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR378">2013</a></span>). Further, although early studies could not find any <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> mutations in other types of solid tumors (Bleeker et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR19">2009</a></span>; Kang et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR154">2009</a></span>), recurrent mutations have since been identified in chondrosarcoma (56%), cholangiocarcinoma (23%), melanoma (10%), and prostate cancer (2%) (Amary et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR3">2011</a></span>; Borger et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR22">2012</a></span>; Ghiam et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR98">2012</a></span>; Shibata et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR290">2011</a></span>).</p><p class="Para">Soon after the discovery of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> mutations in glioma, recurrent mutations of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> were also identified in AML (Green and Beer, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR102">2010</a></span>; Mardis et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR203">2009</a></span>). Similar to glioma, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH1</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH2</em> mutations are mutually exclusive, though the mutational frequency of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> in AML is much lower (23%) (Chou et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR50">2011b</a></span>; Ward et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR343">2010</a></span>). In contrast, the utility of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> mutation status as an independent prognostic marker in AML is less clear. In a convincing cohort of 493 adult patients with AML, Chou and colleagues found that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH1</em> mutation had no impact on OS (Chou et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR48">2010</a></span>). Still, other studies have suggested a more disparate role, with mutation in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH1</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH2</em> conferring poor and improved prognosis, respectively (Chou et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR50">2011b</a></span>; Schnittger et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR279">2010</a></span>). Notably, although another large cohort of 805 patients found that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> mutation did not correlate with prognosis, a subset of patients with <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> mutation and CN-AML, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">NPM1</em> mutation, and no <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">FLT3-ITD</em> did show significantly reduced OS and RFS (Paschka et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR240">2010</a></span>). Therefore, it is likely that the use of prognostic subsets—<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> status along with other genetic markers—may improve the utility of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> status as a biomarker in AML. Other features that correlate with <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> mutation status include normal karyotype, intermediate-risk cytogenetics, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">NPM1</em> mutation, and M1 AML subtype (Chou et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR48">2010</a></span>; Schnittger et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR279">2010</a></span>).</p><p class="Para">To date, practically all <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> mutations found in cancer are heterozygous and highly recurrent. Amino acid substitutions at residues IDH1-R132, IDH2-R172 and IDH2-R140Q are the most common, with considerable variability at R132 (R/H, R/C, R/S, R/G, R/L, R/V) and R172 (R/G, R/M, R/K) (Balss et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR10">2008</a></span>; Yan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR363">2009</a></span>). The remarkable absence of any frameshift or nonsense mutations, deletions, or epigenetic silencing provided early evidence that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> mutations were activating (Flanagan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR82">2012</a></span>; Zhao et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR376">2009</a></span>). The R132 residue is located in the active site of IDH1 where it forms 2 hydrogen bonds with α- and β- carboxylate of isocitrate, its substrate (Nekrutenko et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR220">1998</a></span>; Xu et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR359">2004</a></span>). Initially, it was believed that these mutations may be loss-of-function or dominant-negative, as mutant IDH showed a reduced affinity for isocitrate and produced markedly less α-KG and NADPH <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">in vitro</em> (Yan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR363">2009</a></span>; Zhao et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR376">2009</a></span>). However, an <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">in vitro</em> metabolite screen revealed that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> mutations are neomorphic, producing the novel oncometabolite 2-hydroxyglutarate (2-HG) through heterodimer formation with the remaining wild-type IDH1. This was also verified in patient samples, with a strong correlation between the amount of 2-HG in tumor tissue and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH1</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">2</em> mutation status (Dang et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR58">2009</a></span>; Ward et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR343">2010</a></span>). In fact, 2-HG levels are increased 10–100 fold in patient sera and can be used to reliably diagnose <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> status and monitor response to therapy, though this application may be restricted to myeloid neoplasms (Capper et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR34">2012</a></span>; DiNardo et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR65">2013</a></span>; Ward et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR343">2010</a></span>).</p><p class="Para">The effects of mutant IDH are pleiotropic and affect numerous cell processes including DNA methylation, histone methylation, HIF1a signaling, collagen synthesis, and metabolism (Cairns and Mak, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR31">2013</a></span>). Remarkably, α-KG levels are unchanged in mutant <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> AML and glioma (Dang et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR58">2009</a></span>; Gross et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR104">2010</a></span>), and it is now clear that 2-HG-mediated inhibition of 2-OG-dependent dioxygenases is the dominant mechanism by which <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> mutations are oncogenic (Xu et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR360">2011</a></span>). Early data from the Cancer Genome Atlas project first identified the glioma hypermethylator phenotype (G-CIMP) in GBM and its association with <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> mutations (Laffaire et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR172">2011</a></span>; Noushmehr et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR229">2010</a></span>). Following this, Figueroa and colleagues demonstrated that 2-HG inhibition of the α-KG-dependent enzyme TET2 actively generates the hypermethylator phenotype in AML. Further, they showed that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> mutations are mutually exclusive in AML, result in overlapping methylation signatures, and impair HSC differentiation in 32D myeloid cells (Figueroa et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR81">2010</a></span>). Similarly, work from our lab demonstrated that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH1</em> mutation directly causes the G-CIMP phenotype, reduces global 5-hmC through TET2 inhibition, results in hypermethylation of the repressive histone marks H3K9 and H3K27, and blocks differentiation (Turcan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR320">2012</a></span>). It is important to highlight that widespread loss of 5-hmC is an additional epigenetic hallmark in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> or <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TET2</em> mutated cancers, including melanoma, and that reestablishment of the 5-hmC landscape can suppress tumor invasion and growth in both melanoma cells and a zebrafish model (Lian et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR184">2012</a></span>). Interestingly, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em>-associated increases in histone methylation are likely due to 2-HG-mediated inhibition of the Jumonji C (JmjC)-domain-containing histone demethylases (Lu et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR192">2012</a></span>). Still, others have proposed additional mechanisms in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em>-mutated cancers such as HIF1a stabilization through PHD inhibition, altered ECM structure due to decreased hydroxylation of collagen, and possible metabolic shifts in NADP/NADPH ratio (Sasaki et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR275">2012</a></span>; Zhao et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR376">2009</a></span>).</p><p class="Para">Recently, several exciting studies have shed light on novel mechanisms by which <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> mutations initiate malignant transformation and how underlying mechanisms may be exploited for therapeutic gain. In mouse models of leukemia and melanoma, IDH mutants accelerated cell cycle transition by activation of the MAPK/ERK pathway and repression of tumor suppressors <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">CDKN2A</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">CDKN2B</em> (Chaturvedi et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR40">2013</a></span>; Shibata et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR290">2011</a></span>). Although several studies have noted that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> mutations cause increased colony formation in soft agar and enhanced proliferation, two mouse models for leukemia found that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> mutation primes cells by inducing an MDS- or MPN-like state. However, combining <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH1</em> mutants with HOXA9, or <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH2</em> mutants with FLT3 or NRAS, was sufficient to initiate transformation (Chaturvedi et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR40">2013</a></span>; Chen et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR42">2013a</a></span>; Xu et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR360">2011</a></span>). This may be cancer-specific though, as mutant IDH2 alone was recently shown to be sufficient to induce sarcoma formation in mice, at least in one model system (Lu et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR193">2013</a></span>). Regardless, the primary mechanism underlying IDH-induced oncogenesis in several model systems is a block in cell differentiation (Pirozzi et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR250">2013</a></span>). Both groups showed restoration of differentiation and increased apoptosis following treatment with IDH inhibitor HMS-101 or Brd4 inhibitor JQ1. Similarly, Losman and colleagues showed that IDH mutant leukemic transformation is specific to the (R)-enantiomer of 2-HG, which can independently promote cytokine independence and block differentiation. Again, this transformation was reversible with IDH inhibitor AGI-5198 (Losman et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR190">2013</a></span>). Lastly, IDH inhibition can reverse novel EMT-associated expression patterns, though a lengthy delay to phenotypic change suggests more stable epigenetic alterations may be to blame (Grassian et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR101">2012</a></span>).</p><p class="Para">Despite the targeted nature of IDH inhibitors, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> mutation likely unleashes epigenetic marks that are themselves selectable, such as DNA methylation. There is thus interest in the therapeutic potential of 5-azacytidine (5-aza) and decitabine (DAC), due to the complex downstream effects of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH</em> mutations on the cancer methylome. In order to determine if broader epigenetic therapies could “unlock” glioma initiating cells (GIC) from a dedifferentiated state, we treated both wild-type and mutant IDH cell lines with DAC and/or IDH inhibitor AGI-5198. Along with Borodovsky and colleagues, our group found that hypomethylating agents potently induce differentiation, impair colony formation, and suppress <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">in vivo</em> growth in IDH mutant cells only (Borodovsky et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR23">2013</a></span>; Turcan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR321">2013</a></span>). This demonstrates that mutant IDH-induced DNA methylation likely plays a role in maintaining the self-renewal capacity of glioma tumor initiating cells. Similar effectiveness has also been seen in leukemia and chondrosarcoma (Chaturvedi et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR40">2013</a></span>; Chen et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR42">2013a</a></span>; Lu et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR193">2013</a></span>). Interestingly, IDH inhibition using the mutant IDH1 inhibitor AGI-5198 was not nearly as effective, which suggests that broader epigenetic therapies may be necessary to reverse more permanent changes induced by long exposure to mutant IDH. In addition, combination therapy with other IDH affected processes such as histone hypermethylation may have a role and warrant further investigation.</p></section></div></section><section id="Sec6" tabindex="-1" class="Section1 RenderAsSection1"><h2 class="Heading">HISTONE METHYLATION</h2><div class="content"><p class="Para">Histone methylation is a reversible process that takes place at the amino acid side chains of lysine, arginine, and histidine residues. Lysine methylation on histones H3 and H4 is the best characterized and catalyzed by the lysine methyltransferases (KMTs) through the required methyl group donor SAM. All of the KMTs except DOTL1/KMT4 have a catalytically active SET domain and are highly specific to both histone residue and degree of methylation (mono- vs. di- vs. tri-methylation) (Feng et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR80">2002</a></span>; Rea et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR259">2000</a></span>). Generally, methylation at H3K4, H3K36, and H3K79 corresponds to euchromatic or transcriptionally active regions of the genome, whereas methylation at H3K9, H3K27, and H4K20, is associated with heterochromatic regions and gene silencing. In addition, each residue is capable of four methylated states: unmethylated or mono-/di-/tri-methylated. This provides further regulatory diversity in the histone code. For example, H3K4me2/3 is found at TSSs of active genes, whereas H3K4me1 tends to localize to enhancer regions (Greer and Shi, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR103">2012</a></span>; Kampranis and Tsichlis, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR150">2009</a></span>).</p><p class="Para">In contrast, the lysine-specific demethylases (KDMs) work in opposition to the KMTs through the catalytic removal of methylation marks on histone tails. The two families of KDMs responsible are the (FAD)-dependent amine oxidases and the larger JmjC-containing family of α-KG/Fe(II)-ion dependent oxygenases (Shi and Whetstine, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR287">2007</a></span>). KDM1A/B (LSD1/2) and KDM5A-D (JARID1A-D) catalyze demethylation at H3K4, whereas KDM2A/B (JHDM1A/B) and KDM4A-C (JMJD2A-C) target H3K36, leading to repressed gene transcription at these sites. Alternatively, transcriptional activation is induced in part by demethylation at H3K9 and H3K27 by KDM1A, KDM3A-C (JHDM2A-C), or KDM4A-D and KDM6A/B (UTX/JMJD3), respectively (Varier and Timmers, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR327">2011</a></span>). Due to the broad and essential nature of these epigenetic marks across the genome, genetic aberrations of histone modifiers have powerful effects on vital cellular processes such as differentiation and cell cycle control, among others.</p><section id="Sec7" tabindex="-1" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2"><h3 class="Heading">Writers (KMTs)</h3><section id="Sec8" tabindex="-1" class="Section3 RenderAsSection3"><h4 class="Heading">EZH2</h4><p class="Para">EZH2/KMT6 is the enzymatic component of the polycomb repressor complex 2 (PRC2), which is responsible for methylation at H3K27 and subsequent gene silencing (Kirmizis et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR160">2004</a></span>). Other essential subunits of the PRC2 complex through which EZH2 interacts include embryonic ectoderm development (EED), suppressor of zeste 12 homologue (SUZ12), and RBAP48/RBBP4. Collectively, these polycomb group (PcG) proteins have been shown to regulate vital cellular processes including differentiation, cell identity, stem-cell plasticity, and proliferation (Margueron and Reinberg, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR205">2011</a></span>; Shih et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR291">2012</a></span>). As a result, aberrations in any PRC2 component can have powerful physiologic consequences on the cell.</p><p class="Para">Alterations in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EZH2</em> were first discovered in breast and prostate cancer, where amplification and overexpression first implied it may function as an oncogene (Kleer et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR161">2003</a></span>; Varambally et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR325">2002</a></span>; Yang and Yu, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR366">2013</a></span>). This finding was validated both <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">in vitro</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">in vivo</em>, with <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EZH2</em> overexpression proving sufficient to drive proliferation in cancer cells and transform primary fibroblasts (Bracken et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR26">2003</a></span>; Croonquist and Van Ness, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR54">2005</a></span>). Recent sequencing studies have identified numerous mutations of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EZH2</em> in a variety of leukemias and lymphomas, including follicular lymphoma (FL; 7%–22%), diffuse large B-cell lymphoma (DLBCL; 14%–21.7%), high grade B-cell lymphoma (18%), MDS/MPN (6%–13%), CMML (11.1%), T-ALL, and AML (Abdel-Wahab et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR2">2011</a></span>; Bodor et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR20">2011</a></span>; Capello et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR33">2011</a></span>; Ernst et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR76">2010</a></span>; Grossmann et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR105">2011</a></span>; Lohr et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR188">2012</a></span>; Makishima et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR196">2010</a></span>; Morin et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR215">2010</a></span>; Nikoloski et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR227">2010</a></span>; Ryan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR272">2011</a></span>; Zhang et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR375">2012</a></span>). Interestingly, frequent missense and truncating mutations were observed, which generated some confusion in the field about whether <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EZH2</em> could possess both pro- and anti-oncogenic functions. Clinically, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EZH2</em> mutations seem to commonly predict poor prognosis—worse OS/leukemia-free survival (LFS), high-risk IPSS score—especially in myeloid malignancies (Guglielmelli et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR108">2011</a></span>; Khan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR156">2013</a></span>; Nikoloski et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR227">2010</a></span>). Additionally, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EZH2</em> mutation associates with <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">BCL2</em> rearrangement in FL and germinal center B-cell like DLBCL (GCB-DLBCL) and is notably absent from activated B-cell like DLBCL (ABC-DLBCL) (Beguelin et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR13">2013</a></span>; Morin et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR215">2010</a></span>; Ryan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR272">2011</a></span>).</p><p class="Para">One landmark finding that emerged from the wealth of recent sequencing data was the identification of the highly recurrent heterozygous Y641 mutation (Y/F, Y/N, Y/H, Y/C) in FL, DLBCL, and other lymphoid neoplasms (Bodor et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR21">2013</a></span>; Bodor et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR20">2011</a></span>; Morin et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR215">2010</a></span>). Initially thought to be inactivating due to reduced catalytic ability against a short H3-like peptide <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">in vitro</em>, Y641 mutant EZH2 exhibited a powerful gain-of-function phenotype when incubated against the entire nucleosomal unit (Sneeringer et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR297">2010</a></span>). In addition, Sneeringer and others have observed a powerful synergy between the wild-type and Y641 forms of EZH2 both <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">in vitro</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">in vivo</em>. Whereas EZH2 is very efficient at catalyzing monomethylation of H3K27 but not di-/tri-methylation, EZH2 Y641 shows enhanced ability for di-/tri- methylation at H3K27 (Ryan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR272">2011</a></span>; Wigle et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR350">2011</a></span>; Yap et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR367">2011</a></span>). Similar findings have also been observed with A677G and A687V mutant EZH2, though these are far less prevalent in cancer (Majer et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR195">2012</a></span>; McCabe et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR208">2012a</a></span>). Since almost all <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EZH2</em> gain-of-function mutations are heterozygous, the overall consequence of these mutations is a cooperative and thereby efficient silencing of genes associated with the repressive H3K27 mark.</p><p class="Para">Recent studies have shown that mutant EZH2-driven tumors can be effectively targeted with small molecule inhibitors. Knutson and colleagues were the first to describe potent phenotypic effects in lymphoma cell lines, following treatment with the SAM-competitive EZH2 inhibitor (EPZ005687) (Knutson et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR162">2012</a></span>). This inhibitor was highly selective, inducing cell death in mutant EZH2-expressing cells only. As expected, these cells showed global reduction of the H3K27me2/me3 histone mark and significant enrichment of cell cycle gene sets by GSEA. The more recent EZH2 inhibitor GSK126 was also highly selective for mutant EZH2 lymphoma cells <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">in vivo</em> and led to increased activation of known EZH2 target genes, such as <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TXNIP</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TNFRSF21</em> (McCabe et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR209">2012b</a></span>). Although <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EZH2</em> mutation alone may be insufficient to induce development of B-cell lymphoma, new evidence suggests it functions as a master regulator of GCB phenotype through repression of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">CDKN1A</em>, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IRF4</em>, and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">PRDM1</em> (Beguelin et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR13">2013</a></span>). Due to frequent activation of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EZH2</em> in lymphoma, these new targeted therapies hold exciting promise in the clinic.</p></section><section id="Sec9" tabindex="-1" class="Section3 RenderAsSection3"><h4 class="Heading">SETD2</h4><p class="Para">The major KMT responsible for H3K36 trimethylation is SETD2/KMT3A, which is a novel candidate tumor suppressor gene (TSG) (Edmunds et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR71">2008</a></span>). Gene deletions in clear cell renal cell carcinoma (ccRCC)-derived cell lines are common, reduced expression is seen in breast tumors, and loss is associated with decreased H3K36 trimethylation (Duns et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR69">2012</a></span>; Duns et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR68">2010</a></span>; Newbold and Mokbel, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR223">2010</a></span>). <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SETD2</em> mutations are quite common in ccRCC (7.4%–11.6%), pediatric high-grade glioma (HGG; 15%), and adult HGG (8%) (Cancer Genome Atlas Research Network, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR32">2013</a></span>; Dalgliesh et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR56">2010</a></span>; Duns et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR68">2010</a></span>; Fontebasso et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR84">2013</a></span>; Hakimi et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR114">2013b</a></span>; Varela et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR326">2011</a></span>). Almost all of the mutations characterized so far are frameshift or nonsense and therefore truncating, further suggesting <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SETD2</em> may be an important TSG in select malignancies (Hakimi et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR113">2013a</a></span>). Furthermore, Hakimi and colleagues found that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SETD2</em> mutations were significantly associated with worse cancer-specific survival (CSS) in ccRCC (Hakimi et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR114">2013b</a></span>). Though phenotypic effects of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SETD2</em> inactivation have not been clarified, recent research showed that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SETD2</em> loss triggers MSI and can increase genome-wide mutation rates through alterations in H3K36 methylation (Li et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR182">2013a</a></span>; Schmidt and Jackson, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR278">2013</a></span>).</p></section><section id="Sec10" tabindex="-1" class="Section3 RenderAsSection3"><h4 class="Heading">MLLs</h4><p class="Para">The mammalian mixed lineage leukemia (MLL) family of genes encodes a series of active (MLL1–4/KMT2A–D) and inactive (MLL5/KMT2E) KMTs, which have all been implicated in cancer. MLL1–4 are responsible for methylation at H3K4 and share a common core formed by WDR5, RbBP5, Dpy-30, and Ash2L (Varier and Timmers, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR327">2011</a></span>). Notably, MLL1–2 form a complex along with the menin (MEN1) tumor suppressor and recent evidence shows that H3K27 demethylase KDM6A/UTX can complex with MLL2–4 (Hughes et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR131">2004</a></span>; Yokoyama and Cleary, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR370">2008</a></span>; Yokoyama et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR371">2005</a></span>).</p><p class="Para">The earliest known alterations in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MLL</em> family genes involved frequent rearrangements of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MLL1</em> at 11q23, with recombination involving more than 40 different partner genes and occurring in 60%–80% of infants with ALL or AML (Dimartino and Cleary, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR64">1999</a></span>; Pais et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR236">2005</a></span>; Thirman et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR311">1993</a></span>). Since then, several missense and truncating mutations have been identified in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MLL1</em> in bladder, lung, and breast cancer (Gui et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR109">2011</a></span>; Kan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR151">2010</a></span>). Although <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MLL1</em> acts as a dominant oncogene in liquid tumors, these new discoveries suggest a different recessive role for <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MLL1</em> in some solid tumors (Krivtsov and Armstrong, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR169">2007</a></span>). Infrequent truncating mutations have also been noted in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MLL4</em> in medulloblastoma and head and neck squamous cell carcinoma (HNSCC), suggesting a minor but alternative role for this family member as well (Pugh et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR256">2012</a></span>; Stransky et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR300">2011</a></span>).</p><p class="Para">Notably, recently, many new mutations have been identified in both <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MLL2</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MLL3</em>, showing diversity of both mutation and tumor type. <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MLL2</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MLL3</em> mutations are frequently nonsense or frameshift, resulting in a truncated protein lacking the active SET domain (Morin et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR216">2011</a></span>; Parsons et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR239">2011</a></span>; Pasqualucci et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR242">2011b</a></span>; Pugh et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR256">2012</a></span>; Stransky et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR300">2011</a></span>). Along with <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MLL1</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">4</em>, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MLL2</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">3</em> mutations suggest a dual role for MLL family proteins in oncogenesis, which may depend heavily on cellular context. In addition, mutations have been discovered in numerous cancers, occasionally at high frequency, including colon (<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MLL3</em>, 14%–17%), DLBCL (<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MLL2</em>, 24%–32%), FL (<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MLL2</em>, 89%), AML, breast, GBM, RCC, prostate, pancreatic, bladder, medulloblastoma, and HNSCC (Balakrishnan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR9">2007</a></span>; Dalgliesh et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR56">2010</a></span>; Gui et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR109">2011</a></span>; Li et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR183">2013b</a></span>; Lindberg et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR187">2013b</a></span>; Mann et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR199">2012</a></span>; Morin et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR216">2011</a></span>; Parsons et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR238">2008</a></span>; Parsons et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR239">2011</a></span>; Pasqualucci et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR242">2011b</a></span>; Pugh et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR256">2012</a></span>; Sjoblom et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR295">2006</a></span>; Stransky et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR300">2011</a></span>; Vakoc et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR322">2009</a></span>; Watanabe et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR345">2011</a></span>). Unfortunately, functional data remains sparse and the importance of these mutations has yet to be characterized. Watanabe and colleagues made the interesting observation that in colorectal carcinoma, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MLL3</em> mutations were associated with increased MSI, though no mechanism has been proposed (Watanabe et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR345">2011</a></span>). Regardless, they are intriguing candidates for further study in cancer, especially since MLL family proteins are important regulators of HOX proteins and differentiation (Wang et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR337">2009a</a></span>).</p></section><section id="Sec11" tabindex="-1" class="Section3 RenderAsSection3"><h4 class="Heading"><em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">NSD1</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">2</em></h4><p class="Para">Histone-lysine N-methyltransferase <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">NSD2</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MMSET</em> was first implicated in cancer as a target for rearrangement [t(4;14)(p16.3;q32)] in 15%–20% of multiple myeloma (MM) patients (Chesi et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR46">1998</a></span>). This translocation results in aberrant upregulation of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">NSD2</em>, which first suggested that it may be an oncogene. Subsequent work has shown that knockdown in MM KMS11 cells leads to apoptosis and re-expression of wild-type NSD2 causes increased proliferation (Martinez-Garcia et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR206">2011</a></span>). Furthermore, overexpression of wild-type <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">NSD2</em> is sufficient to transform <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">NSD2</em><sup>-/-</sup> cancer cells <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">in vivo</em> and in mouse embryonic fibroblasts (MEFs) (Kuo et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR171">2011</a></span>). Functionally, interactions with HDAC1/2 and catalytic activity at H3K4 and H4K20 have been proposed, though these may be minor (Marango et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR201">2008</a></span>).
