It is now clear that the NSD2-catalyzed conversion of unmethylated H3K36 to mono- or di-methylated forms, with concomitant decreases in H3K27me3, is the dominant mechanism driving <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">NSD2</em>-associated oncogenic reprogramming (Kuo et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR171">2011</a></span>; Li et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR180">2009</a></span>). In fact, Kuo and colleagues demonstrated that NSD2 SET catalytic activity is required for transcriptional activation at several oncogenic loci (<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TGFA</em>, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MET</em>, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">PAK1</em>, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">RRAS2</em>). Pathway analyses have also identified the following as significantly altered in mutant NSD2 tumors: TP53 pathway, cell cycle, DNA repair, focal adhesion, and Wnt (Kuo et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR171">2011</a></span>; Martinez-Garcia et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR206">2011</a></span>).</p><p class="Para">Recently, sequencing projects revealed the presence of a highly recurrent mutation in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">NSD2</em> (E1099K), which is present in 7.5% of pediatric B-ALL and other lymphoid neoplasms (Jaffe et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR140">2013</a></span>; Oyer et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR235">2013</a></span>). These studies showed that NSD2 E1099K leads to enhanced colony formation in soft agar and expected increases and decreases in H3K36me2 and H3K27me3, respectively. This new discovery has exciting therapeutic potential—similar to the activating mutations in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EZH2</em> described above. Additionally, the related KMT <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">NSD1</em> was recently discovered to harbor point mutations in multiple cancers, including HNSCC and AML (Dolnik et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR66">2012</a></span>; Yan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR364">2011</a></span>). If these mutations prove to be similarly activating, both NSD members will represent completely novel areas of epigenetic regulation through which small molecule targeted inhibition could be useful.</p></section></section><section id="Sec12" tabindex="-1" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2"><h3 class="Heading">Erasers (KDMs)</h3><section id="Sec13" tabindex="-1" class="Section3 RenderAsSection3"><h4 class="Heading">KDM6A</h4><p class="Para">Among the first cancer-associated mutations in KDMs that were identified were those in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM6A</em> following sequencing of 1,390 patient tumor samples (van Haaften et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR324">2009</a></span>). Remarkably, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM6A</em> mutations were found to be widespread across both solid and liquid tumors, including AML, chronic myelogenous leukemia (CML), T-ALL, MM, Hodgkin’s lymphoma (HL), TCC, breast, colon, esophageal, pancreas, endometrial, GBM, small cell lung cancer (SCLC), non-small cell lung cancer (NSCLC), and RCC (Dalgliesh et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR56">2010</a></span>; Gui et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR109">2011</a></span>; Mann et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR199">2012</a></span>; Mar et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR200">2012</a></span>; Ross et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR269">2013</a></span>; van Haaften et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR324">2009</a></span>). Since then, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM6A</em> mutations have also been discovered in other tumors such as prostate cancer, medulloblastoma, and adenoid cystic carcinoma (Ho et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR124">2013</a></span>; Lindberg et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR186">2013a</a></span>; Robinson et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR266">2012</a></span>). Although these mutations occur at low frequency in most cancers, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM6A</em> mutations in bladder carcinoma are quite common (20%–29%) (Gui et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR109">2011</a></span>; Poon et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR254">2013</a></span>; Ross et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR269">2013</a></span>). Furthermore, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM6A</em> mutations in bladder carcinoma associate with earlier grade and are inversely correlated with stage (Gui et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR109">2011</a></span>). Therefore, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM6A</em> inactivation may be a powerful driver and early event in bladder oncogenesis. However, whether <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM6A</em> mutation status holds significant prognostic value in cancer is yet to be determined.</p><p class="Para">Despite infrequent inactivation in many cancers, mutation and functional data have established that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM6A</em> is a <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">bona fide</em> tumor suppressor gene. KDM6A is a 1401 amino acid protein with several N-terminal tetratricopeptide-repeat (TPR) domains and a single C-terminal Jumonji C (JmjC) domain (Shpargel et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR292">2012</a></span>). Early sequencing efforts revealed that a majority of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM6A</em> mutations are either frameshift or nonsense, and since most occur before the active JmjC demethylase domain, they are most likely inactivating. To test this hypothesis, van Haaften and colleagues re-expressed wild-type <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM6A</em> in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM6A</em>-deleted cell lines and observed markedly reduced proliferation (van Haaften et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR324">2009</a></span>). Similarly, we recently showed that cancer-specific missense mutations in the JmjC domain can abrogate this growth suppressive effect and may even contribute to a dominant proliferative phenotype. Furthermore, following overexpression, these tumor-specific mutants exhibited reduced demethylase activity at the repressive chromatin mark H3K27me3 (Ho et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR124">2013</a></span>). Dysregulation of methylation at H3K27 may have important consequences in cancer, as demethylation of H3K27 at HOX genes is required for proper differentiation. Interestingly, it has also been reported that KDM6A binds directly to the HOXB1 locus and that its activation requires KDM6A catalytic activity (Christensen et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR51">2007</a></span>; Morales Torres et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR213">2013</a></span>). In addition to regulation through HOX gene targets, KDM6A catalytic activity also activates RB pathway genes through HBP1 to further influence differentiation and cell cycle control (Herz et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR123">2010</a></span>; Wang et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR339">2010</a></span>).</p><p class="Para">In addition to contributing to tumor suppression via its catalytic domain, KDM6A also has important demethylase-independent roles in cancer. A recent study found that conditional inactivation of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM6A</em> in a mouse model did not change global levels of H3K27me3, though it did contribute to a MDS-like phenotype and reduced migration of HSCs (Thieme et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR310">2013</a></span>). Furthermore, it is also known that KDM6A can bind to KMTs MLL2–4 to promote H3K4 methylation independent of its catalytic domain and that demethylase-inactive KDM6A is sufficient to induce differentiation (Cho et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR47">2007</a></span>; Issaeva et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR136">2007</a></span>; Lee et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR175">2007</a></span>; Morales Torres et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR213">2013</a></span>). In fact, Wang and colleagues mapped the chromatin occupancy of KDM6A, H3K4me2, and H3K27me3 in primary human fibroblasts and discovered that 62% of KDM6A target genes are enriched for univalent H3K4me2 (Wang et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR339">2010</a></span>). Therefore, KDM6A has at least two independent, yet complimentary, mechanisms for shaping the epigenetic landscape in cancer. Indeed, one study found that inactivating mutations in the catalytic JmjC domain caused increased growth yet also led to simultaneous increases and decreases in H3K27me3 and H3K4me1, respectively (Herz et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR123">2010</a></span>). As a result, it is likely that the <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM6A</em>-associated phenotypes in cancer are diverse and are linked to the type and location of each driving mutation.</p></section><section id="Sec14" tabindex="-1" class="Section3 RenderAsSection3"><h4 class="Heading">Other KDMs</h4><p class="Para">Although <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM6A</em> mutations are the most prevalent and best characterized among the KDMs, several others have been identified as significantly mutated across cancer, albeit at low frequency (Cerami et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR36">2012</a></span>; Gao et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR94">2013</a></span>; Parsons et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR239">2011</a></span>; Pasqualucci et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR242">2011b</a></span>). Uniquely, ccRCC harbors mutations in many of the KDMs, including <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM1A</em>, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM2B</em>, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM3A</em>, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM3B</em>, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM4A</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">B</em>, and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM5C</em> (Dalgliesh et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR56">2010</a></span>; Hakimi et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR113">2013a</a></span>; Larkin et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR174">2012</a></span>; Shi et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR288">2011</a></span>). The natural function of these KDMs is still being determined, but these studies and others suggest <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM1A</em>, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM4A</em>–<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">C</em>, and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM5B</em> are putative oncogenes, whereas <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM6A</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">B</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM3B</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">C</em> are tumor suppressors. Also, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM2A</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">B</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM5A</em> seem likely to be both pro- and anti-oncogenic, depending on context (Rotili and Mai, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR270">2011</a></span>). More recently, Niu and colleagues provided the first <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">in vivo</em> evidence that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM5C</em> serves as a tumor suppressor following VHL loss in ccRCC and that cancer-specific mutations were inactivating. Furthermore, they demonstrated that HIF2a binds directly to KDM5C, targeting KDM5C to demethylate H3K4me3 at HIF-repressed gene loci (Niu et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR228">2012</a></span>). It will be exciting to determine if some of these mutations are true oncogenic drivers. Additionally, if gain-of-function mutants are identified in KDM oncoproteins, these may be prime candidates for existing KDM inhibitors or new targeted therapies (Rotili and Mai, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR270">2011</a></span>).</p></section></section></div></section><section id="Sec15" tabindex="-1" class="Section1 RenderAsSection1"><h2 class="Heading">HISTONE ACETYLATION</h2><div class="content"><p class="Para">Lysine residues on histone tails may also undergo another form of covalent modification through the addition of an acetyl functional group. This process uniquely results in the neutralization of charge normally associated with lysine residues, which weakens the electrostatic interaction between histones and negatively charged DNA. As a result, it is believed that histone acetylation primarily results in a more “open” chromatin configuration, serving as a “mark” of active gene transcription. Several ChIP-seq studies have now confirmed this, showing localization of acetylated histones at enhancers, promoters, and even throughout the transcribed region of active genes (Dawson and Kouzarides, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR60">2012</a></span>; Heintzman et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR119">2007</a></span>; Wang et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR336">2008</a></span>). In addition to altering the chromatin state directly, these specific histone “marks” further act to recruit other remodelers containing “reader” bromodomains and tandem plant homeodomain (PHD) fingers (Taverna et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR306">2007</a></span>).</p><p class="Para">The process of histone acetylation is carried out by the lysine acetyltransferase (KAT) enzymes, of which there are two major classes: Type-A, which are usually found in the nucleus and Type-B, which are cytoplasmic and act on free histones. Dynamic regulation of acetylation is also catalyzed by the histone deacetylase (HDAC) enzymes, which oppose the actions of KATs and remove acetyl groups from histone tails. Interestingly, these enzymes are capable of modifying other non-histone proteins—including p53, Rb, and MYC—and have additional roles as transcriptional cofactors, which has led to many challenges in determining their specific roles in cancer and other disease processes (Dawson and Kouzarides, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR60">2012</a></span>; Iyer et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR139">2004</a></span>).</p><section id="Sec16" tabindex="-1" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2"><h3 class="Heading">Writers (CREBBP and EP300)</h3><p class="Para">CREB-binding protein (CREBBP) and E1A binding protein p300 (EP300) are structurally distinct from other KATs and have unique broad substrate specificity, including the ability to acetylate all four histones <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">in vitro.</em> In fact, both proteins are highly conserved, with 75% similarity across their entire length and 63% homology at the amino-acid level (Iyer et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR139">2004</a></span>; Shiama, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR289">1997</a></span>). Not surprisingly, many functional similarities exist. Both proteins engage in several diverse functions, including chromatin remodeling via KAT activity, acetylation of association proteins (p53, Rb, E2F), and the ability to act as scaffolds for transcription factors and other transcriptional machinery (Bannister and Kouzarides, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR11">1996</a></span>; Gu and Roeder, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR107">1997</a></span>; Nakajima et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR219">1997</a></span>). Despite being some of the earliest epigenetic modifiers identified, their roles in both normal physiology and disease are just beginning to be appreciated.</p><p class="Para">Both <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">CREBBP</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EP300</em> have long been linked to cancer, though the specific roles they play have been harder to elucidate. Suspicion that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">CREBBP</em> may be a tumor suppressor first arose in the mid-1990s, when heterozygous germline mutations were identified in the setting of Rubinstein-Taybi syndrome, a developmental disorder with an increased prevalence of cancer, including leukemia and lymphoma (Petrij et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR249">1995</a></span>). Around the same time, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EP300</em> was first shown to bind to the E1A viral oncoprotein, suggesting it may also function as a tumor suppressor (Eckner et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR70">1994</a></span>). Soon after these discoveries, the first genetic alteration of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">CREBBP</em> in cancer was identified in M4/M5 AML subtypes, albeit a rare t(8,16)(p11,p13) translocation that fuses the <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MOZ</em> gene with the N-terminus of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">CREBBP</em> (Borrow et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR24">1996</a></span>; Panagopoulos et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR237">2001</a></span>). Interestingly, reports of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MOZ</em>-<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EP300</em> translocations do exist, though these events may be exceedingly rare (Lai et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR173">1985</a></span>). Though a few early studies identified low frequency <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EP300</em> mutations in colorectal carcinoma, breast cancer, and gastric cancer, the full spectrum of mutational inactivation of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">CREBBP</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EP300</em> would not be fully evident until the genomics era.</p><p class="Para">Genomic and exomic sequencing data from the past several years have revealed that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">CREBBP</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EP300</em> inactivation via mutation is more widespread and frequent than previously thought. For example, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">CREBBP</em> mutations have now been described in NHL (21%), DLBCL (29%), FL (32.6%), TCC (13%), ACC (7%), and relapsed ALL (18.3%), with <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EP300</em> mutations occurring slightly less frequently in NHL (7%), DLBCL (10%), FL (8.7%), TCC (13%), ACC, and relapsed ALL (Gui et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR109">2011</a></span>; Ho et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR124">2013</a></span>; Morin et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR216">2011</a></span>; Mullighan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR217">2011</a></span>; Pasqualucci et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR241">2011a</a></span>). Additionally, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">CREBBP</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EP300</em> are collectively mutated in up to 18% of SCLC (Peifer et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR245">2012</a></span>). Interestingly, mutations in both genes are mutually exclusive, suggesting functional equivalency, at least in part. Additionally, the majority of mutations are heterozygous, indicating that both genes most likely function as haploinsufficient tumor suppressors. In line with this, an earlier mouse study showed that heterozygous <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">CREBBP</em> loss led to increased neoplasia over wild-type mice (Kung et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR170">2000</a></span>). In almost all of these studies, mutations strongly clustered in the catalytic KAT domain, several of which exhibit reduced acetyltransferase ability <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">in vitro</em> at H3K18 and in the non-histone substrates Bcl6 and p53 (Mullighan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR217">2011</a></span>; Pasqualucci et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR241">2011a</a></span>; Peifer et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR245">2012</a></span>). Though the targets of CREBBP/p300 are diverse, it seems likely that disruption of acetyltransferase ability can be a main contributor to tumor formation.</p><p class="Para">Despite the identification and initial functional characterization of these tumor-specific mutations, the physiologic consequences leading to oncogenesis remain obscure. For example, in SCLC, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">CREBBP</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EP300</em> mutations do not lead to any obvious concerted shifts in gene expression, even though several reported mutations are inactivating <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">in vitro</em> (Peifer et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR245">2012</a></span>). Due to the diverse roles of CREBBP and p300, it is possible that some inactivating mutations exert phenotypic consequences through other non-epigenetic mechanisms. For example, a more recent discovery showed that both proteins exhibit acetyltransferase specificity for histones H3 and H4 at double-strand break (DSB) sites, facilitating the recruitment of the SWI/SNF chromatin remodeling complex (Ogiwara et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR230">2011</a></span>). Also, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">CREBBP</em> KAT mutations favor constitutive activity of the Bcl6 oncogene over p53, which may be alone sufficient to promote tumorigenesis. Lastly, paradoxical roles for <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">CREBBP</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EP300</em> have been described recently. For example, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EP300</em> is actually upregulated in melanoma cell lines, and inhibition of KAT function <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">in vitro</em> reduces melanoma tumor cell growth (Yan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR365">2013</a></span>). Additional experiments will be necessary to help identify functional consequences of these mutations and in what specific contexts these two KATs function as tumor suppressors.</p></section><section id="Sec17" tabindex="-1" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2"><h3 class="Heading">Erasers (HDACs)</h3><p class="Para">In contrast to acetyltransferases, the 18 member HDAC family is responsible for the removal of acetyl groups from lysine residues on histone tails. Similar to KATs, HDACs have a wide range of protein targets, and are also known to deacetylate nonhistone substrates (Ellis et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR73">2009</a></span>). The HDAC family has four major classes, with class I (nucleus), II (nucleus and cytoplasm), and IV requiring the zinc ion for catalytic activity. Class III (sirtuins) are catalytically active in the absence of zinc and share almost no homology with the other HDACs (New et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR222">2012</a></span>). Notably, several HDACs have been implicated in cancer. Specifically, functional experiments have revealed that these HDACs are pro-oncogenic, with increased apoptosis and reduced proliferation (Class I) or reduced angiogenesis and cell migration (Class II) following specific HDAC knockdown (Ellis et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR73">2009</a></span>). Most importantly, this role is congruent with their acetyltransferase counterparts, the KATs, which have been characterized as tumor suppressors (see above).</p><p class="Para">In the past decade, much work has been done to develop targeted inhibitors of HDACs, since they are now established oncogenes in many cellular contexts. These HDAC inhibitors (HDACi) work to prevent histone deacetylation, facilitating a more open chromatin configuration and leading to increased gene transcription. Vorinostat, the first FDA approved HDACi, is already in use for select neoplasms, and other more selective HDACi show promising efficacy in early trials at reducing cell growth and increasing apoptosis (Witt et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR354">2009</a></span>). Despite the substantial therapeutic potential for HDACi, there is a need to determine biomarkers for treatment response and resistance.</p><p class="Para">Sequencing data from the past several years has identified inactivating mutations in several HDACs, which may influence the effectiveness of HDAC inhibitor therapy and predict overall response in patients (You and Jones, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR372">2012</a></span>). Specifically, mutations have now been reported in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">HDAC2</em> (colon), <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">HDAC4</em> (breast), and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">HDAC9</em> (prostate) (Berger et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR15">2011</a></span>; Ropero et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR267">2006</a></span>; Sjoblom et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR295">2006</a></span>). Of these, the recurrent frameshift mutation of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">HDAC2</em> in exon 1 has been the most extensively characterized. This mutation is incredibly common in colon cancer (21%), resulting in a premature stop codon and loss of measurable <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">HDAC2</em> expression in 83% of mutant tumors (Ropero et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR267">2006</a></span>). Further, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">HDAC2</em> mutations are enriched in MSI colon cancers (43%) (Hanigan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR116">2008</a></span>). Importantly, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">in vitro</em> work showed that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">HDAC2</em>-deficient cells were resistant to HDACi via trichostatin A, exhibiting no hyperacetylation at target histones H3 and H4 and no reduction in proliferation compared to wild-type <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">HDAC2</em>-expressing cells (Ropero et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR267">2006</a></span>). Recent work has identified the pro-apoptotic gene <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">APAF1</em> as a likely target for repression by HDAC2, providing a specific mechanism for both HDACi efficacy and resistance in HDAC2 mutant cells, where HDACi does not appreciably alter <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">APAF1</em> levels (Hanigan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR116">2008</a></span>). Lastly, although there is an important role for using HDAC mutation status as a predictor of HDACi treatment response, Ropero and colleagues have further shown that the <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">HDAC2</em> mutation itself can cause changes in gene expression, actively leading to increased levels of multiple pro-tumorigenic proteins (Ropero et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR268">2008</a></span>).</p></section></div></section><section id="Sec18" tabindex="-1" class="Section1 RenderAsSection1"><h2 class="Heading">CHROMATIN REMODELING</h2><div class="content"><p class="Para">In addition to gene regulation via covalent histone tail modifications, the ATP-dependent chromatin remodelers also shape chromatin structure and thereby affect gene expression patterns. Several multi-unit effectors share this responsibility, including SWI/SNF, ISWI, INO80, SWR1, and NURD/Mi2/CDH complexes. In the past several years, protein components of the SWI/SNF complex have been found to be frequently inactivated in cancer, and subsequent work has solidified their status as <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">bona fide</em> epigenetic tumor suppressors (Wilson and Roberts, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR351">2011</a></span>).</p><section id="Sec19" tabindex="-1" class="Section2 RenderAsSection2"><h3 class="Heading">SWI/SNF complex</h3><p class="Para">The SWI/SNF complex consists of one or two mutually exclusive catalytic ATPases (SMARCA2/BRM or SMARCA4/BRG1), a group of conserved core subunits (SMARCB1/SNF5, SMARCC1/BAF155, SMARCC2/BAF170), and other variant subunits (Wilson and Roberts, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR351">2011</a></span>). Two important SWI/SNF complexes implicated in cancer are the BAF and PBAF complexes, which contain the mutually exclusive ARID1A or ARID1B subunits and PBRM1 or BRD7 subunits, respectively (Reisman et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR261">2009</a></span>; Wang et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR342">2013</a></span>). Collectively, SWI/SNF complexes remodel chromatin through the mobilization of nucleosomes both by sliding and by the ejection/insertion of histone octomers (Saha et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR273">2006</a></span>). Through these mechanisms, the SWI/SNF complexes have powerful effects on transcriptional regulation, serving an important role in development through the coordinate activation and repression of critical gene expression programs. Importantly, specificity is most likely achieved through the unique combinatorial assembly of the SWI/SNF complex, facilitated by the sheer size and diversity of the protein subunit repertoire (Wang et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR335">1996</a></span>).</p><section id="Sec20" tabindex="-1" class="Section3 RenderAsSection3"><h4 class="Heading">ARIDs</h4><p class="Para">The AT-rich interactive-containing domain (ARID) gene superfamily consists of seven members (ARID1–5), of which the following have now been implicated in cancer: <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID1A</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">BAF250a</em>, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID1B</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">250b</em>, and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID2</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">BAF200</em>. Mutations in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID1A</em> are the most widely reported in the literature, with remarkable frequency, first reported in ovarian clear cell carcinoma (OCCC; 50%) and endometrioid carcinoma (30%) (Bosse et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR25">2013</a></span>; Jones et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR147">2010</a></span>; Wiegand et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR348">2010</a></span>). Mutations in other cancers exist, including medulloblastoma, breast, lung adenocarcinoma, ACC, hepatocellular carcinoma (HCC), gastric, pancreatic, and neuroblastoma (Fujimoto et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR88">2012</a></span>; Ho et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR124">2013</a></span>; Sausen et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR276">2013</a></span>; Wu and Roberts, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR355">2013</a></span>; Zang et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR374">2012</a></span>). Interestingly, the majority of mutations are heterozygous, truncating, and evenly spread along the protein, suggesting a possible role as a haploinsufficient tumor suppressor. Functional studies have confirmed this, noting increased proliferation and colony formation, impaired differentiation, and decreased apoptosis following partial <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID1A</em> knockdown (Gao et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR93">2008</a></span>; Luo et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR194">2008</a></span>; Nagl et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR218">2007</a></span>; Zang et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR374">2012</a></span>). Correspondingly, re-expression of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID1A</em> decreases cell proliferation (Zang et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR374">2012</a></span>). In addition, a role in differentiation seems likely, though conflicting data on the specific consequences of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID1A</em> inactivation has been complicated through varying technical approaches and model systems (Wu and Roberts, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR355">2013</a></span>).</p><p class="Para">Little is currently known about how <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID1A</em> inactivation leads to malignant transformation through SWI/SNF chromatin remodeling, though several intriguing possibilities exist. Both ARID1A and ARID1B provide unique and mutually exclusive specificities for SWI/SNF recruitment to chromatin (Wilson and Roberts, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR351">2011</a></span>). Interestingly, with ARID1A, this process is at least partially independent of its ARID domain, which binds DNA in a non-specific manner only (Dallas et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR57">2000</a></span>). Instead, ARID1A likely contributes to specific recruitment of SWI/SNF by binding transcription factors and transcriptional coactivator/corepressor complexes, including nuclear hormone receptors (Nie et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR225">2000</a></span>; Trotter and Archer, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR318">2004</a></span>). In fact, Inoue and colleagues showed that re-expression of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID1A</em> in a breast cancer cell line augments transcriptional activation through glucocorticoid receptors, estrogen receptor, and androgen receptor (Inoue et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR134">2002</a></span>). This specificity is likely due to the presence of several nuclear hormone receptor binding sites near the C-terminus of ARID1A (Nie et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR225">2000</a></span>). In light of this, the impressive prevalence of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID1A</em> mutations in hormone-responsive neoplasms (ovary, breast) is likely to be more than just coincidence.</p><p class="Para">The clinical utility of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID1A</em> mutation status is currently unclear, due to a limited number of sufficiently powered studies. However, Sausen and colleagues recently found that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID1A</em> or <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID1B</em> mutations associate with worse OS in patients with neuroblastoma, and a previous breast cancer study noted that decreased <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID1A</em> expression can confer worse prognosis (Mamo et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR197">2012</a></span>; Sausen et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR276">2013</a></span>). Several other intriguing observations have been noted, including a tendency toward co-occurrence between mutations in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID1A</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">CTNNB1</em> (β-catenin) or PI3K-Akt pathway alterations, as well as a mutual exclusivity between <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID1A</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TP53</em> mutations (Bosse et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR25">2013</a></span>). Whether <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID1A</em>-associated phenotypes arise through additional epigenetic means, such as reported binding partners HDAC1/2, its ubiquitin ligase activity at H2B K120, or by increased MSI, remains to be determined (Bosse et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR25">2013</a></span>; Li et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR181">2010</a></span>).</p><p class="Para">Mutations in other ARID family members <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID1B</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID2</em> have now been reported, though investigation into their clinical and functional relevance is in its infancy. <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID1B</em> mutations occur at moderate frequency in neuroblastoma (7%) and HCC (6.7%), with sporadic mutations identified in breast, gastric, and pancreatic cancers too (Fujimoto et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR88">2012</a></span>; Sausen et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR276">2013</a></span>; Shain et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR283">2012</a></span>; Wang et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR340">2011</a></span>). These mutations are usually frameshift and hemizygous, suggesting it may be a tumor suppressor, like <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID1A</em>. More intriguing has been the discovery of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID2</em> mutations in HCC (5.8%–6.5%), with strong enrichment in HCV-associated HCC (14%) (Fujimoto et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR88">2012</a></span>; Zhao et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR377">2011</a></span>). <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID2</em> seems to mediate anti-proliferative signaling by binding to IFN-inducible promoters to remodel chromatin in response to interferon signaling. As such, mutations in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID2</em> may render IFN-related immune processes incapacitated in the setting of HCV infection, leading to accelerated tumorigenesis. More recently, mutations in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ARID2</em> were identified in NSCLC (7.3%), making this gene one of the most frequently mutated in this type of cancer (Manceau et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR198">2013</a></span>).</p></section><section id="Sec21" tabindex="-1" class="Section3 RenderAsSection3"><h4 class="Heading">SMARCs</h4><p class="Para">SWI/SNF-related matrix-associated actin-dependent regulator of chromatin (SMARC) genes, also known as BRG1-associated factors, are among the most frequently altered and best characterized chromatin remodelers in cancer. These genes encode several SWI/SNF proteins including the catalytic ATPase subunits (either SMARCA2 or SMARCA4), a group of conserved core subunits (e.g. SMARCB1, SMARCC1, and SMARCC2), and variant subunits (e.g. SMARCE1) (Wilson and Roberts, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR351">2011</a></span>). Tumor-specific mutations in the following SMARCs are especially common: <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCB1</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SNF5</em>, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCA2</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">BRM</em>, and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCA4</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">BRG1</em> (Shain et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR283">2012</a></span>). Alterations in these critical chromatin remodelers have profound effects on vital processes, such as differentiation, cell proliferation, and metastasis.</p><p class="Para"><em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCB1</em> is inactivated via biallelic alterations—deletion and mutation (truncating, missense)—in nearly all rhabdoid tumors (RTs; 98%), an especially lethal cancer that predominantly affects young children (Sievert et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR293">2009</a></span>; Versteege et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR330">1998</a></span>). Recent exomic sequencing data has identified mutations in other tumors and pre-malignant lesions, such as sarcoma, gastric carcinoma, schwannomatosis, meningioma, chordoma, and hepatoblastoma (Christiaans et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR52">2011</a></span>; Hulsebos et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR132">2007</a></span>; Kim et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR159">2013</a></span>; Kreiger et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR168">2009</a></span>; Mobley et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR212">2010</a></span>; Trobaugh-Lotrario et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR317">2009</a></span>). Mouse models for <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCB1</em> loss have verified a tumor suppressive role for this protein, with homozygous loss leading to frequent formation of lymphomas and RT-like tumors at only 11 weeks, which is notably faster than <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TP53</em>-inactivated models of sarcoma (Guidi et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR110">2001</a></span>; Roberts et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR265">2000</a></span>; Wilson and Roberts, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR351">2011</a></span>). Interestingly, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCB1</em>-deleted mice do not develop tumors in the absence of SWI/SNF ATPase SMARCA4 (Wang et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR338">2009b</a></span>), suggesting that oncogenesis following <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCB1</em> inactivation is due to aberrant residual activity of SMARCA4-containing SWI/SNF complexes. Additionally, global gains of H3K27me3 following loss of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCB1</em> may directly contribute to tumorigenesis, as simultaneous <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EZH2</em> loss protects against transformation. In fact, EZH2 elevation occurs immediately following <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCAB1</em> inactivation, and re-expression of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCB1</em> evicts PcG proteins from the <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">CDKN2A</em> tumor suppressor locus, activating transcription of this important tumor suppressor (Kia et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR158">2008</a></span>; Wilson et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR352">2010</a></span>). This evidence provides both a necessary mechanism and a possible therapeutic target (EZH2) in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCB1</em>-inactivated tumors.</p><p class="Para">Further mechanistic insights have yielded many other potential therapeutic avenues in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCB1</em>-mutated tumors. For example, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCB1</em> plays a critical role in cell cycle control. Mouse models have shown that coinactivation with Rb or p16 does not accelerate tumorigenesis, and simultaneous loss of cyclin D1 protects against tumor formation (Isakoff et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR135">2005</a></span>; Oruetxebarria et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR234">2004</a></span>; Tsikitis et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR319">2005</a></span>). In this vein, cyclin D1 inhibitors may have surprising effectiveness in these patients. Furthermore, direct binding to Myc has also been reported, along with increased Myc expression following <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCB1</em> loss (Cheng et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR44">1999</a></span>; Tsikitis et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR319">2005</a></span>). Though the efficacy of synthetic lethal approaches in targeting Myc-driven tumorigenesis has yet to be established, these strategies may be worthwhile therapeutic candidates. In addition to cell cycle dysregulation, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCB1</em> inactivation leads to alterations in cell differentiation, specifically by increasing hedgehog signaling through the transcription factor Gli1 (Jagani et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR141">2010</a></span>). Thus, inhibitors of Gli1 provide a fourth potential target for therapy in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCB1</em>-inactivated tumors. Lastly, increased RHOA activity and subsequent cell migration was observed following knockdown of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCB1</em>, though therapeutic possibilities of this mechanism remain unexplored (Caramel et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR35">2008</a></span>).</p><p class="Para"><em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCA4</em>, one of two SWI/SNF ATPase subunits, was first identified as a potential tumor suppressor in NSCLC, where expression is lost in 15%–50% of all tumors (Fukuoka et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR91">2004</a></span>; Reisman et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR260">2003</a></span>). <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCA4</em> mutations have also been noted in 35% of NSCLC cell lines, as well as in medulloblastoma, Burkitt lymphoma, melanoma, HCC, ccRCC, HNSCC, RT, pancreatic, breast, and prostate cancer (Cancer Genome Atlas Research Network, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR32">2013</a></span>; Endo et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR75">2013</a></span>; Love et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR191">2012</a></span>; Medina et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR210">2008</a></span>; Oike et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR231">2013</a></span>; Shain and Pollack, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR282">2013</a></span>). The majority of mutations and deletions are homozygous, though loss of only one allele may be sufficient to drive tumorigenesis. For instance, 10% of mice heterozygous for <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCA4</em> develop mammary tumors without spontaneous loss of the second allele (Bultman et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR28">2000</a></span>; Bultman et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR29">2008</a></span>). Functional studies have so far identified dual roles for SMARCA4 in both differentiation and cell adhesion/migration. In embryonic stem cells (ESCs), inactivation of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCA4</em> leads to defective self-renewal and promotes differentiation, while overexpression enhances the epigenetic reprogramming of fibroblasts into induced pluripotent stem (iPS) cells, possibly through increased OCT4 binding to target genes (Singhal et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR294">2010</a></span>). In addition, SMARCA4 has been shown to promote osteoblast differentiation (Flowers et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR83">2009</a></span>). Alternatively, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCA4</em> overexpression in cervical cancer cell lines leads to increased ROCK1 and stress fiber formation, which is reversible upon <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCA4</em> knockdown (Asp et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR5">2002</a></span>). Through this latter mechanism, SMARCA4 may serve to modulate cell migration and reduce the potential for invasion.</p><p class="Para"><em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCA2</em>, the other ATPase subunit of the SWI/SNF complex, is also a <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">bona fide</em> tumor suppressor with frequent loss of expression via epigenetic silencing and more recently, mutational inactivation (Oike et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR231">2013</a></span>). Mouse models have shown that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCA2</em> deficiency results in proliferative abnormalities, including increased overall weight and tissue-specific increased growth (Reyes et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR263">1998</a></span>). These mice exhibit increased cell proliferation in the prostate as well as androgen independence (Shen et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR286">2008</a></span>). In a similar lung model, heterozygous or homozygous loss of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCA2</em> led to increased tumor formation (Glaros et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR99">2007</a></span>). Other studies have proposed another mechanism for enhanced tumorigenicity following <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCA2</em> or <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCA4</em> loss, specifically through the induction of an epithelial-mesenchymal transition (EMT) phenotype via the transcription factor ZEB1 (Matsubara et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR207">2013</a></span>; Sanchez-Tillo et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR274">2010</a></span>). Clinically, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCA2</em> inactivation may predict poor prognosis, as loss of expression in both NSCLC and HCC correlates with worse OS (Endo et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR75">2013</a></span>; Fukuoka et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR91">2004</a></span>; Reisman et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR260">2003</a></span>). Recently, we identified somatic nonsynonymous mutations in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCA2</em> (5%) following whole-exome and whole-genome sequencing of 60 patients with ACC (Ho et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR124">2013</a></span>). All of these mutations were located in a region encoding the Helicase C domain, which is involved in regulating gene transcription. Other <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCA2</em> mutations have also been reported in another cohort of patients with melanoma (Nikolaev et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR226">2012</a></span>). Despite epigenetic silencing being the dominant mechanism for <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCA2</em> inactivation, specific mutations may also prove important in the pathogenesis of certain cancers.</p></section><section id="Sec22" tabindex="-1" class="Section3 RenderAsSection3"><h4 class="Heading">PBRM1</h4><p class="Para">Mutations in chromatin state regulators are very common in ccRCC, with <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">PBRM1</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">BAF180</em> mutations noted in 29%–41% of all ccRCCs (Hakimi et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR113">2013a</a></span>; Varela et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR326">2011</a></span>). In fact, the only gene altered more frequently in this malignancy is <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">VHL</em>, a well-characterized driver of these cancers. In addition, lower frequency mutation of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">PBRM1</em> has also been described in many other neoplasms, including DLBCL, HNSCC, chronic lymphoid leukemia (CLL), gastric, pancreatic, and breast cancer (Shain and Pollack, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR282">2013</a></span>; Xia et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR358">2008</a></span>). Many of these mutations are truncating—frameshift and nonsense—though a sizable number are also missense, evenly distributed across the protein (Hakimi et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR113">2013a</a></span>). It is most likely that these mutations are inactivating, as loss of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">PBRM1</em> expression and gene deletion are noted in several cancers and cell lines (Wang et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR341">2012</a></span>). Further, functional studies have shown that re-expression of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">PBRM1</em> in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">PBRM1</em>-deficient cells induces cell growth arrest in G<sub>1</sub> phase, specifically through binding of the <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">CDKN1A</em> promoter, leading to induction of p21 (Xia et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR358">2008</a></span>). PBRM1 contains six tandem bromodomains that bind acetylated histone, two BAH domains that mediate protein-protein interactions, and a HMG domain to bind nucleosomal DNA, all of which provide functional specificity to SWI/SNF remodelers (Wilson and Roberts, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR351">2011</a></span>).</p><p class="Para">Exactly how these mutations affect epigenetic reprogramming has yet to be determined, though they are likely pleiotropic, diverse, and dependent on location and type of mutation. It is clear that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">PBRM1</em> inactivation does have clinical consequences, at least in ccRCC. For example, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">PBRM1</em> mutations have been shown to correlate to worse clinical outcomes (Pawlowski et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR244">2013</a></span>). Also, patients with <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">PBRM1</em> inactivation are more likely to present with stage III or IV disease, increased tumor size, low differentiation grade, increased perinephric or lymphatic invasion, and are less likely to have an “incidentaloma” at diagnosis (da Costa et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR55">2013</a></span>; Hakimi et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR113">2013a</a></span>; Pawlowski et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR244">2013</a></span>). In addition, smaller tumors (&lt;4 cm) with <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">PBRM1</em> mutations are more likely to exhibit stage II pathologic features. Most interestingly, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">PBRM1</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">BAP1</em> mutations are mutually exclusive in ccRCC, and in a side-by-side comparison, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">PBRM1</em> mutation correlates with improved OS over <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">BAP1</em> alterations (Kapur et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR155">2013</a></span>; Pena-Llopis et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR246">2013</a></span>).</p></section><section id="Sec23" tabindex="-1" class="Section3 RenderAsSection3"><h4 class="Heading">BRD7</h4><p class="Para">BRD7 is a PBAF-specific chromatin remodeler that is frequently deleted in breast cancer. Recently, low frequency mutations in this gene have been identified in several cancers (Cerami et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR36">2012</a></span>; Forbes et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR85">2011</a></span>). For some time, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">BRD7</em> was believed to be an important SWI/SNF tumor suppressor that functions mainly as a cofactor for p53 to activate oncogene-induced senescence (OIS) (Burrows et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR30">2010</a></span>). In fact, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">BRD7</em> was first identified in a loss-of-function screen for genes required for p53-dependent OIS. Drost and colleagues showed that p53 required BRD7 for OIS—and vice versa—and that p53 directly interacts with the N-terminus of BRD7, upstream of its bromodomain. Furthermore, BRD7 was required for induction of several p53 target genes, including <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">CDKN1A</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MDM2</em>, and was capable of direct binding to their associated promoters (Drost et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR67">2010</a></span>). This mechanism likely explains the reported ability of BRD7 to inhibit cell cycle progression from G<sub>1</sub> to S phase, though direct binding at the <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">E2F3</em> promoter may also facilitate this (Peng et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR247">2006</a></span>).</p><p class="Para">Though this mechanism is likely important in certain cancers, the diversity of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">BRD7</em> phenotypes may be dependent on the unique molecular architecture found within different tissues. For example, a recent study focusing on epithelial ovarian carcinoma showed that <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">BRD7</em> can act as a tumor suppressor independent of p53 activity, possibly by sequestering β-catenin in the cytoplasm. These data were further supported by a β-catenin responsive TCF-reporter assay <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">in vitro</em> and the presence of decreased <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">BRD7</em> expression in high-grade epithelial ovarian serous carcinoma clinical specimens (Bae et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR8">2013</a></span>). Regardless of the mechanism, overexpression of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">BRD7</em> has powerful tumor suppressive effects in cancer cell lines, resulting in decreased cell viability and reduced invasion/migration, independent of p53 status. Lastly, BRD7 may also influence covalent histone modifications themselves, with knockdown resulting in decreased acetylation at H3K9 and direct binding observed <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">in vitro</em> at histone residue H3K14 (Bae et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR8">2013</a></span>; Peng et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR247">2006</a></span>). Future studies are necessary to determine which mutations have functional consequences and whether any associated therapeutic vulnerabilities can be exploited.</p></section></section></div></section><section id="Sec24" tabindex="-1" class="Section1 RenderAsSection1"><h2 class="Heading">HISTONES</h2><div class="content"><p class="Para">An exciting development in the past few years has been the identification of recurrent mutations in genes encoding the histones themselves. Histone mutations in cancer were first discovered following massive exomic sequencing efforts in colon cancer (<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">HIST1H1B</em>, 4%) and NHL (<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">HIST1H1C</em>, 7%), though frequencies were low and no recurrent mutations were identified (Morin et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR216">2011</a></span>; Sjoblom et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR295">2006</a></span>). However, a recent pair of pediatric glioma studies revealed highly recurrent heterozygous mutations in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">HIST1H3B</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">H3F3A</em>, which encode histones H3.1 and H3.3, respectively. Collectively, Wu et al. and Schwartzentruber et al. reported common <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">H3F3A</em> mutations in diffuse intrinsic pontine glioma (DIPG; 78%) and pediatric GBM (22%–31%), with <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">HIST1H3B</em> mutations almost exclusive to DIPG (18%). Most striking is the location of these mutations at amino acid residues K27M and G34 (G/R, G/V), which are at or near critical sites for post-translational modification via methylation and acetylation on the histone tail (e.g. H3K27, H3K36) (Schwartzentruber et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR280">2012</a></span>; Wu et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR357">2012</a></span>). This observation, along with the mutually exclusive and monoallelic nature of the K27 and G34 mutations, led to subsequent investigations into the clinical and biologic consequences of these potentially gain-of-function mutations.</p><p class="Para">Currently, K27M and G34R/V mutations have been found almost exclusively in pediatric HGGs, including CNS-PNET (11%, G34R), though sporadic mutations at these residues have been recently noted in osteosarcoma (G34W) and MDS (K27N) (Attieh et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR6">2013</a></span>; Gessi et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR97">2013</a></span>; Joseph et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR148">2013</a></span>). In fact, the remarkable prevalence of these mutations in pediatric HGGs has enabled a thorough examination into the clinical significance of these events. For example, despite the mutually exclusive nature of K27M and G34 mutations, which implies a common pathway or biological process, there are important differences. First, several studies have noted that the K27M mutation predicts both younger age and worse OS as compared to G34 and wild-type H3.3 (Chan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR38">2013a</a></span>; Khuong-Quang et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR157">2012</a></span>; Sturm et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR302">2012</a></span>). In one study, G34 mutations were actually associated with improved OS when compared to wild-type H3.3 (Sturm et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR302">2012</a></span>). Second, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">H3F3A</em> mutations are frequently exclusive with <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH1</em> mutations and commonly co-occur with <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ATRX</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">DAXX, PDGFRA,</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">TP53</em> alterations, especially in G34 mutant tumors (Schwartzentruber et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR280">2012</a></span>; Sturm et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR302">2012</a></span>). Overlap between alterations in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">H3F3A</em> and <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">ATRX</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">DAXX</em> is particularly interesting, since both have been linked to increased CNVs and alternative lengthening of telomeres (ALT) in tumor specimens (Yuen and Knoepfler, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR373">2013</a></span>). Third, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">H3F3A</em> mutations predict tumor anatomic location—K27M and G34 mutant GBMs occur in midline and hemispheric areas of the brain, respectively (Chan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR38">2013a</a></span>; Schwartzentruber et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR280">2012</a></span>). Likewise, a groundbreaking study by Sturm and colleagues revealed distinct epigenetic subtypes of GBM, with K27 and G34 mutant tumor samples exhibiting independent gene expression and methylation profiles (Bjerke et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR17">2013</a></span>; Schwartzentruber et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR280">2012</a></span>; Sturm et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR302">2012</a></span>). Interestingly, these profiles may also explain the anatomic preferences; the G34 mutant transcriptomic signature resembles embryonic regions of the neocortex and striatum, whereas the K27M signature is more similar to embryonic regions of the striatum and thalamus. Lastly, these epigenetic signatures harbor important differences in marker genes, with DNA hypermethylation and associated silencing at <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">FOXG1</em> (K27M), <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MICA</em> (K27M), or <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">OLIG1</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">2</em> (G34) and elevated expression of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">PDGFRA</em> (K27M) (Bender et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR14">2013</a></span>; Sturm et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR302">2012</a></span>). Collectively, these changes in the methylome and transcriptome define the different H3.3 mutant GBM subtypes, correlate to anatomic tumor locations, and indicate potentially different cell origins or initiation events in tumorigenesis. Further, they provide mechanistic insight into <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">H3F3A</em>-induced oncogenesis.</p><p class="Para">Global DNA hypomethylation and decreases in genome-wide histone methylation suggest that mutant H3.3 functions in a dominant-negative manner (Bender et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR14">2013</a></span>; Chan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR38">2013a</a></span>; Lewis et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR177">2013</a></span>; Sturm et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR302">2012</a></span>). Several groups have now shown that K27M mutant patient samples and cell lines exhibit globally reduced H3K27 di-/tri-methylation and at many loci, DNA hypomethylation (Bender et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR14">2013</a></span>; Chan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR38">2013a</a></span>; Lewis et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR177">2013</a></span>). Further, forced overexpression of H3.3 K27M in isogenic GBM cells, 293T cells, and MEFs causes reduced H3K27me2/me3 (but not changes in monomethylation) as well as modest increases in H3K27ac, two features of transcriptionally active chromatin (Bender et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR14">2013</a></span>; Chan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR39">2013b</a></span>). The major mechanism for loss of histone di-/tri-methylation is K27M-mediated inhibition of the PRC2 complex through direct binding of KMT EZH2 at the catalytic site, as well as binding to PRC2 cofactor SUZ12 (Bender et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR14">2013</a></span>; Chan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR38">2013a</a></span>; Lewis et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR177">2013</a></span>). In fact, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">in vitro</em> KMT assays revealed that K27M reduces KMT activity by 40%–70% and EZH2 catalytic activity up to 85%, with no loss of PRC2-associated KDM activity (e.g KDM6A/B) (Bender et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR14">2013</a></span>). Somewhat contradictory to this finding, ChIP-seq has revealed that hundreds of gene loci are also enriched for H3K27me3 and EZH2 in K27M mutant tumors or following overexpression of K27M, and analysis of differential expression shows increased gene transcription at these loci (Bender et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR14">2013</a></span>; Bjerke et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR17">2013</a></span>; Chan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR38">2013a</a></span>). The mechanism underlying these paradoxical changes in H3K27 methylation and associated transcription changes is currently unknown (Chan et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR39">2013b</a></span>).</p><p class="Para">In contrast to the K27M mutation, broad changes in DNA methylation and histone marks underlying G34R/V induced tumorigenesis are less clear. Further, though investigators have shown that G34R/V mutations disrupt methylation at nearby amino acid residue K36, our understanding of the mechanisms leading to malignant transformation is limited. Despite this, analysis of differential gene expression between H3.3 G34R/V mutant and wild-type tumors has already identified some interesting markers (e.g. OLIG1/2), as stated above. As such, the defining feature of H3.3 mutant tumors is the aberrant activation and repression of numerous genes that uniquely contribute to oncogenesis. Understanding the constellation of changes unique to H3.3 mutants should allow identification of additional subtypes and driver genes. Most exciting is the recent identification of G34-induced activation of <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MYCN</em> in pediatric GBM cell line KNS42 (Bjerke et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR17">2013</a></span>). This well-characterized oncogene is potentially druggable through synthetic lethal means, via aurora kinase A (AURKA), or JQ1 inhibition (Huang and Weiss, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR130">2013</a></span>). Implementing this strategy in patients harboring <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">H3F3A</em> G34 mutations could have immense therapeutic benefit. Similarly, identifying and characterizing other differentially expressed genes in <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">H3F3A</em> mutant pediatric gliomas could present new ways of treating this very difficult disease.</p></div></section><section id="Sec25" tabindex="-1" class="Section1 RenderAsSection1"><h2 class="Heading">SUMMARY AND PERSPECTIVES</h2><div class="content"><p class="Para">Understanding the role of altered epigenetic states has long been a fundamental goal in cancer research. It is now well-documented that distinct chromatin states are both necessary and sufficient to drive tumor formation, sustain increased cellular growth, and encourage metastatic dissemination (Baylin et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR12">2001</a></span>; Jones and Baylin, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR145">2002</a></span>). However, the link between classical genetics and cancer epigenomics has only recently been explored. In recent years, a plethora of mutations have been discovered in chromatin modifier genes. Studies now show that these alterations have profound effects on the cancer epigenome, leading to potent oncogenic transcriptional programs.</p><div class="Para">It has become abundantly clear that mutations in epigenetic modifiers are both incredibly diverse and ubiquitous in cancer. In fact, sequencing data has revealed that mutations exist in genes involved in nearly all aspects of epigenetics, including DNA methylation, covalent histone modification, and chromatin remodeling (Fig. <span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Fig2">2</a></span>). Several neoplasms rely on few, yet powerful, mutations in select genes to drive tumorigenesis through an altered epigenome, at least in part (e.g. <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH1</em>/<em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">2</em> in LGG, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">MLL2</em> in FL, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">SMARCB1</em> in RT). Remarkably, some cancers have such a wide range and high prevalence of alterations in chromatin modifiers (ACC, ccRCC, TCC) that they may be driven primarily through epigenetic means. Interestingly, mutations in regulators of DNA methylation have a strong cancer-specific prevalence (glioma, leukemia), whereas genetic alterations in histone modifiers (e.g. <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">KDM6A</em>) are widespread across many cancers, though the significance of this is incompletely understood.<figure class="Figure" id="Fig2"><div class="MediaObject"><a href="https://static-content.springer.com/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs13238-014-0031-6/MediaObjects/13238_2014_31_Fig2_HTML.gif" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"><span class="u-screenreader-only">Open image in new window</span><img src="https://static-content.springer.com/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs13238-014-0031-6/MediaObjects/13238_2014_31_Fig2_HTML.gif" alt="" /></a></div><figcaption class="Caption" lang="en"><div class="CaptionContent"><span class="CaptionNumber">Figure 2</span><p class="SimplePara"><strong class="EmphasisTypeBold ">Frequently mutated epigenetic regulators in human cancer</strong>. Select oncogenes and tumor suppressors implicated in altered cancer epigenomes are shown, along with mutation frequency by tumor histology. *Role in oncogenesis (oncogene vs. tumor suppressor) is either mixed or undetermined</p></div></figcaption></figure></div><div class="Para">Although the field of epigenomics in cancer is relatively new, the identification of driver mutations in epigenetic regulator genes has already led to new prognostic and therapeutic advances. New subtypes of cancer have been identified, or at least attributed to previously unknown genetic alterations, allowing a more nuanced approach to treatment. Further, several new targeted therapies are currently in development and show great promise at reversing phenotypes caused by activating mutations in certain genes (e.g. <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">IDH1</em>, <em class="EmphasisTypeItalic ">EZH2</em>). In addition, understanding the epigenetic mechanisms that underlie these alterations has also provided new therapeutic potential, even if the driver mutations cannot be targeted directly (e.g. HDACi, DNMT inhibitors) (Table <span class="InternalRef"><a href="#Tab1">1</a></span>) (Arrowsmith et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR4">2012</a></span>; Foulks et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR86">2012</a></span>; Helin and Dhanak, <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR120">2013</a></span>; New et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR222">2012</a></span>; Plass et al., <span class="CitationRef"><a href="#CR251">2013</a></span>).<div id="Tab1" class="Table"><div class="Caption" lang="en"><div class="CaptionContent"><span class="SimplePara">KDM1A/LSD1</p></td><td><p class="SimplePara">Phase II</p></td></tr><tr><td><p class="SimplePara">ORY-1001</p></td><td><p class="SimplePara">KDM1A/LSD1</p></td><td><p class="SimplePara">Preclinical</p></td></tr><tr><td><p class="SimplePara">AGI-5198</p></td><td><p class="SimplePara">Mutant IDH1</p></td><td><p class="SimplePara">Preclinical</p></td></tr><tr><td><p class="SimplePara">AGI-6780</p></td><td><p class="SimplePara">Mutant IDH2</p></td><td><p class="SimplePara">Preclinical</p></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div><p class="Para">There is no doubt that we are in the midst of an exciting era in both cancer genomics and epigenetics. Investigations focused on the intersection of these two fields have provided remarkable clarity into numerous aspects of cancer biology and novel mechanisms of oncogenesis. Already, great strides have been made to translate these early discoveries into clinical practice, and the characterization of novel mutations in chromatin modifier genes is rapidly accelerating. With the ongoing development of new epigenetic therapies, it is possible that much improved outcomes, at least in certain cancers, may be possible in the near future. Further, an enhanced understanding of the epigenomic alterations associated with these driver gene mutations could lead to the discovery of novel pathways involved in tumorigenesis, which may themselves possess unique vulnerabilities for therapeutic intervention.</p></div></section></div><section id="Notes" class="Section1 RenderAsSection1"><h2 class="Heading">Notes</h2><div class="content"><section><h3 class="Heading">Acknowledgements</h3><p class="SimplePara">We apologize to the many authors whose work we could not cite because of space constraints. <script type="text/javascript">
    window.onload = function() {
    var linkEl = document.querySelector('.js-ctm');


    function viewport() {
        if (document.documentElement.clientWidth < 620) {
            size = 'small';
        }
        else if(document.documentElement.clientWidth < 1075 ) {
            size = 'medium';
        }
        else {
            size = 'wide';
        }
        return size;
    }

    function reportForMouseEvent(linkCssSelector, nolardUrl, experiment, abgroup) {
        var counter = 0;
        $('body').delegate(linkCssSelector, 'click', function() {
            if(counter == 0) {
                reportConversion(nolardUrl, experiment, abgroup);
                counter++;
            }
        });
    }

    function reportParticipation(nolardUrl, experiment, abgroup) {
        $.ajax({ url: nolardUrl + '/participate/' + experiment + '/' + abgroup });
    }

    function reportConversion(nolardUrl, experiment, abgroup) {
        $.ajax({ url: nolardUrl + '/convert/' + experiment + '/' + abgroup });
    }
    };
</script>
